<p><b>ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam</b> or <b>ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm</b> is the second-largest <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">branch</a> of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. It holds that the <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">Islamic prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad</a> designated <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« á¹¬Älib</a> as his <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">successor</a> (<i>khalÄ«fa</i>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imamah_(Shia_doctrine)" title="Imamah (Shia doctrine)">Imam</a> (spiritual and political leader) after him,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> most notably at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Event_of_Ghadir_Khumm" title="Event of Ghadir Khumm">event of Ghadir Khumm</a>, but was prevented from succeeding Muhammad as the leader of the <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> as a result of the choice made by some of <a href="/wiki/Companions_of_the_Prophet" title="Companions of the Prophet">Muhammad's other companions</a> (<i>á¹£aá¸¥Äba</i>) at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saqifah" title="Saqifah">Saqifah</a>. This view primarily contrasts with that of <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">SunnÄ« Islam</a>, whose adherents believe that Muhammad did not appoint a successor before <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_of_Muhammad" title="Death of Muhammad">his death</a> and consider <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">AbÅ« Bakr</a>, who was appointed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a> by a group of senior Muslims at Saqifah, to be the first <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">rightful</a> (<i>rÄshidÅ«n</i>) caliph after Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Adherents of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam are called <b>ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims</b>, <b>ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«tes</b>, or simply <b>ShÄ«Ê¿a</b> or <b>Shia</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam is based on a <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith"><i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> report</a> concerning Muhammad's pronouncement at Ghadir Khumm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito,_John_2002._p._40_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito,_John_2002._p._40-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims believe that <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« á¹¬Älib</a>, Muhammad's cousin and son-in-law, should have been the designated successor to Muhammad as Islam's spiritual and political leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> This belief later developed into the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imamah_(Shia_doctrine)" title="Imamah (Shia doctrine)">Imamah</a>, the idea that certain descendants of Muhammad, the <i><a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">Ahl al-Bayt</a></i>, are rightful rulers or Imams,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> whom ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims believe possess special spiritual and political authority over the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-franc46_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-franc46-8">[8]</a></sup> Although there are many <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches#ShÄ«Ê¿a_Islam" title="Islamic schools and branches">ShÄ«Ê¿a subsects</a>, modern ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam has been divided into two main groupings: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelvers" title="Twelvers">Twelvers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s</a>, with Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿as being the largest and most influential group among ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam is the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches#ShÄ«Ê¿a_Islam" title="Islamic schools and branches">second largest branch of Islam</a>, followed by 10â15% of all Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PEW2009_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-PEW2009-12">[12]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm</a> is the largest branch of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newman2013_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newman2013-13">[13]</a></sup> comprising about 85% of all ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<p>Collectively, adherents of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam are called the <i>ShÄ«Ê¿ah</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">Ø´ÙÙØ¹ÙØ©</span>; <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="/Ê/: 'sh' in 'shy'">Ê</span><span title="/iË/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iË</span><span title="/É/: 'a' in 'about'">É</span></span>/</a></span></span>), which is short for <i>ShÄ«Ê¿atu Ê¿AlÄ«</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">Ø´ÙÙØ¹ÙØ© Ø¹ÙÙÙÙÙ</span>; <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ËÊiËÊatu ËÊaliË/</span>) meaning "followers of Ali", "faction of Ali", or "partisans of Ali";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica738_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica738-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <i>ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«</i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">Ø´ÙÙØ¹ÙÙÙ</span></span>) denotes both the singular noun and the adjective form, while <i>ShÄ«yÄÊ¿</i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">Ø´ÙÙÙØ§Ø¹</span></span>) refers to the plural noun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wehr-498_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-wehr-498-17">[17]</a></sup> <i>ShÄ«Ê¿a</i> or <i>Shia</i> and <i>ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm</i>/<i>ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te</i> or <i>Shiism</i>/<i>Shiite</i> are the forms used in <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_languages" title="European languages">European languages</a> for adherents, mosques, traditions, and things associated with the ShÄ«Ê¿a branch of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term was first used during Muhammad's lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> At present, the word refers to the Muslims who believe that the leadership of the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a> after Muhammad belongs to <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« á¹¬Älib</a>, Muhammad's cousin and son-in-law, and his successors. Nawbakhti states that the term <i>ShÄ«Ê¿a</i> refers to a group of Muslims who at the time of Muhammad and after him regarded Ê¿AlÄ« as the Imam and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Shahrastani" title="Al-Shahrastani">Al-Shahrastani</a> expresses that the term <i>ShÄ«Ê¿a</i> refers to those who believe that Ê¿AlÄ« is designated as the <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">heir</a>, Imam, and caliph by Muhammad<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and that Ê¿AlÄ«'s authority is maintained through his descendants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> For the adherents of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam, this conviction is implicit in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">history of Islam</a>. ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslim scholars emphasize that the notion of authority is linked to the family of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">Abrahamic prophets</a> as the Quranic verses <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D3%3Averse%3D33" rel="nofollow">3:33</a> and <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D3%3Averse%3D34" rel="nofollow">3:34</a> show: <i>"Indeed, God chose Adam and Noah and the family of Abraham and the family of 'Imran over the worlds â (33) Descendants, some of them from others. And God is Hearing and Knowing. (34)"</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beliefs_and_practices">Beliefs and practices</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islamic_beliefs_and_practices" title="Shia Islamic beliefs and practices">Shia Islamic beliefs and practices</a></div>
<p>ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam is the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">second largest branch of Islam</a>, followed by 10â15% of all Muslims,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PEW2009_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-PEW2009-12">[12]</a></sup> considered to be vast and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches#ShÄ«Ê¿a_Islam" title="Islamic schools and branches">inclusive of many different denominations and subgroups</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica738_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica738-15">[15]</a></sup> ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam embodies a completely independent system of religious interpretation and political authority in the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The original ShÄ«Ê¿a identity referred to the followers of Imam Ê¿AlÄ«,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and ShÄ«Ê¿a theology was formulated after the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijra_(Islam)" title="Hijra (Islam)">hijra</a></i> (8th century CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The first ShÄ«Ê¿a governments and societies were established by the end of the 9th century CE. The 10th century CE has been referred to by the scholar of Islamic studies <a href="/wiki/Louis_Massignon" title="Louis Massignon">Louis Massignon</a> as "the Shiite Ismaili century in the history of Islam".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Profession_of_faith_.28Shahada.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Profession_of_faith_(Shahada)">Profession of faith (<i>Shahada</i>)</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kalema-tut-shahadat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Kalema-tut-shahadat.jpg/230px-Kalema-tut-shahadat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Kalema-tut-shahadat.jpg/345px-Kalema-tut-shahadat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Kalema-tut-shahadat.jpg/460px-Kalema-tut-shahadat.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kalema-tut-shahadat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kalema at <a href="/wiki/Qibla" title="Qibla">Qibla</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Ibn_Tulun" title="Mosque of Ibn Tulun">Mosque of Ibn Tulun</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, displaying the phrase <i>Ali-un-Waliullah</i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">Ø¹ÙÙ ÙÙÙ Ø§ÙÙÙ</span></span>: "Ê¿AlÄ« is the <i><a href="/wiki/Wali" title="Wali">Wali</a></i> (custodian) of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>")</div></div></div>
<p>The ShÄ«Ê¿a version of the <i><a href="/wiki/Shahada" title="Shahada">Shahada</a></i>, the Islamic profession of faith, differs from that of the <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">SunnÄ«s</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shahada_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shahada-30">[30]</a></sup> The SunnÄ« version of the <i>Shahada</i> states "There is no god except God, Muhammad is the messenger of God", but to this declaration of faith ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims append the phrase <i>Ali-un-Waliullah</i> (<span title="Arabic-language text"><span dir="rtl" lang="ar">Ø¹ÙÙ ÙÙÙ Ø§ÙÙÙ</span></span>: "Ê¿AlÄ« is the <i><a href="/wiki/Wali" title="Wali">Wali</a></i> (custodian) of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>"). The basis for the ShÄ«Ê¿a belief in Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« á¹¬Älib as the <i>Wali</i> of God is derived from the Quranic verse <a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus%3Atext%3A2002.02.0006%3Asura%3D5%3Averse%3D55" rel="nofollow">5:55</a>, the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verse_of_Wilayah" title="Verse of Wilayah">Verse of Wilayah</a>".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>This additional phrase to the declaration of faith embodies the ShÄ«Ê¿a emphasis on the inheritance of authority through <a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">Muhammad's family and lineage</a>. The three clauses of the ShÄ«Ê¿a version of the <i>Shahada</i> thus address the fundamental Islamic beliefs of <i><a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">Tawá¸¥Ä«d</a></i> (unity and oneness of God), <i><a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">Nubuwwah</a></i> (the <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">prophethood of Muhammad</a>), and <i><a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Shia_doctrine" title="Imamate in Shia doctrine">Imamah</a></i> (the Imamate, leadership of the faith).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Infallibility_.28Ismah.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Infallibility_(Ismah)">Infallibility (<i>Ismah</i>)</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ismah" title="Ismah">Ismah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Imam_Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib_(A.S.).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Imam_Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib_%28A.S.%29.png/230px-Imam_Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib_%28A.S.%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Imam_Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib_%28A.S.%29.png/345px-Imam_Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib_%28A.S.%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Imam_Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib_%28A.S.%29.png/460px-Imam_Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib_%28A.S.%29.png 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Imam_Ali_ibn_Abi_Talib_(A.S.).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« á¹¬Älib</a>, cousin and son-in-law of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_prophet" title="Islamic prophet">Islamic prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, is credited as the first male convert to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><i>Ismah</i> is the concept of <a href="/wiki/Infallibility" title="Infallibility">infallibility</a> or "divinely bestowed freedom from error and sin" in Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dabashi_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dabashi-32">[32]</a></sup> Muslims believe that Muhammad, along with <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">other prophets and messengers in Islam</a>, possessed <i>ismah</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«</a> ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims also attribute the quality to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imamah_(Shia_doctrine)" title="Imamah (Shia doctrine)">Imams</a> as well as to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimah" title="Fatimah">FÄá¹­imah</a>, daughter of Muhammad, in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Zaydism" title="Zaydism">ZaydÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿as</a>, who don't attribute <i>ismah</i> to the Imams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Though initially beginning as a political movement, infallibility and sinlessness of the Imams later evolved as a distinct belief of (non-ZaydÄ«) ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Schools_of_Islamic_theology#ShÄ«Ê¿a_schools_of_theology" title="Schools of Islamic theology">ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslim theologians</a>, infallibility is considered a rational, necessary precondition for spiritual and religious guidance. They argue that since <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> has commanded absolute obedience from these figures, they must only order that which is right. The state of infallibility is based on the ShÄ«Ê¿a interpretation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_verse_of_purification" title="The verse of purification">the verse of purification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Thus, they are the most pure ones, the only immaculate ones preserved from, and immune to, all uncleanness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> It doesn't mean that supernatural powers prevent them from committing a <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_sin" title="Islamic views on sin">sin</a>, but due to the fact that they have absolute belief in God, they refrain from doing anything that is a sin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dabashi_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dabashi-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>They also have a complete knowledge of God's will. They are in possession of all knowledge brought by the <a href="/wiki/Angels_in_Islam" title="Angels in Islam">angels</a> to the prophets (<i>nabÄ«</i>) and the messengers (<i>rÄsÅ«l</i>). Their knowledge encompasses the totality of all times. Thus, they are believed to act without fault in religious matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims regard <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« á¹¬Älib</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">successor of Muhammad</a> not only ruling over the entire <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a> in justice, but also interpreting the Islamic faith, practices, and its esoteric meaning. Hence he was regarded as being free from error and sin (infallible), and appointed by God by divine decree (<i><a href="/wiki/Nass_(Islam)" title="Nass (Islam)">nass</a></i>) to be the first Imam.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Ê¿AlÄ« is regarded as a "perfect man" (<i>al-insan al-kamil</i>) similar to Muhammad, according to the ShÄ«Ê¿a viewpoint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Occultation_.28Ghaybah.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Occultation_(Ghaybah)">Occultation (<i>Ghaybah</i>)</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Occultation_(Islam)" title="Occultation (Islam)">Occultation (Islam)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reappearance_of_Hujjat_Allah_al-Mahdi" title="Reappearance of Hujjat Allah al-Mahdi">Reappearance of Hujjat Allah al-Mahdi</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Major_Occultation" title="Major Occultation">Major Occultation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minor_Occultation" title="Minor Occultation">Minor Occultation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Fourteen_Infallibles" title="The Fourteen Infallibles">The Fourteen Infallibles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jamkaran_Mosque_%D9%85%D8%B3%D8%AC%D8%AF_%D8%AC%D9%85%DA%A9%D8%B1%D8%A7%D9%86_%D9%82%D9%85_21.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Jamkaran_Mosque_%D9%85%D8%B3%D8%AC%D8%AF_%D8%AC%D9%85%DA%A9%D8%B1%D8%A7%D9%86_%D9%82%D9%85_21.jpg/240px-Jamkaran_Mosque_%D9%85%D8%B3%D8%AC%D8%AF_%D8%AC%D9%85%DA%A9%D8%B1%D8%A7%D9%86_%D9%82%D9%85_21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Jamkaran_Mosque_%D9%85%D8%B3%D8%AC%D8%AF_%D8%AC%D9%85%DA%A9%D8%B1%D8%A7%D9%86_%D9%82%D9%85_21.jpg/360px-Jamkaran_Mosque_%D9%85%D8%B3%D8%AC%D8%AF_%D8%AC%D9%85%DA%A9%D8%B1%D8%A7%D9%86_%D9%82%D9%85_21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Jamkaran_Mosque_%D9%85%D8%B3%D8%AC%D8%AF_%D8%AC%D9%85%DA%A9%D8%B1%D8%A7%D9%86_%D9%82%D9%85_21.jpg/480px-Jamkaran_Mosque_%D9%85%D8%B3%D8%AC%D8%AF_%D8%AC%D9%85%DA%A9%D8%B1%D8%A7%D9%86_%D9%82%D9%85_21.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jamkaran_Mosque_%D9%85%D8%B3%D8%AC%D8%AF_%D8%AC%D9%85%DA%A9%D8%B1%D8%A7%D9%86_%D9%82%D9%85_21.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jamkaran_Mosque" title="Jamkaran Mosque">Jamkaran Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Qom" title="Qom">Qom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> is a popular pilgrimage site for ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims. Local belief holds that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hujjat-Allah_al-Mahdi" title="Hujjat-Allah al-Mahdi">12th ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te Imam</a>âthe promised <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a> according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver_Shi%E2%80%99ism" title="Twelver Shiâism">Twelvers</a>âonce appeared and offered prayers at Jamkaran.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Occultation_(Islam)" title="Occultation (Islam)">Occultation</a> is an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">eschatological belief</a> held in various denominations of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam concerning a <a href="/wiki/Messianism" title="Messianism">messianic figure</a>, the hidden and last Imam known as "the <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a>", that one day shall return on Earth and fill the world with justice. According to the doctrine of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm</a>, the main goal of Imam Mahdi will be to establish an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_state" title="Islamic state">Islamic state</a> and to apply <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic laws</a> that were revealed to Muhammad. The Quran doesn't contain verses on the Imamate, which is the basic doctrine of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Some ShÄ«Ê¿a subsects, such as the <a href="/wiki/Zaydism" title="Zaydism">ZaydÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿as</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nizari_Isma%27ilism" title="Nizari Isma'ilism">NizÄrÄ« IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s</a>, don't believe in the idea of the Occultation. The groups which do believe in it differ as to which lineage of the Imamate is valid, and therefore which individual has gone into Occultation. They believe there are many signs that will indicate the time of his return.
</p><p>Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims believe that the prophesied Mahdi and <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Imams" title="Twelve Imams">twelfth Imam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad al-Mahdi">Hujjat Allah al-Mahdi</a>, is already on Earth in Occultation, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reappearance_of_Hujjat_Allah_al-Mahdi" title="Reappearance of Hujjat Allah al-Mahdi">will return at the end of time</a>. <a href="/wiki/Tayyibi_Isma%27ilism" title="Tayyibi Isma'ilism">á¹¬ayyibi IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s</a> and Fatimid/Bohra/<a href="/wiki/Dawoodi_Bohra" title="Dawoodi Bohra">Dawoodi Bohra</a> believe the same but for their 21st á¹¬ayyib, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/At-Tayyib_Abi_l-Qasim" title="At-Tayyib Abi l-Qasim">At-Tayyib Abi l-Qasim</a>, and also believe that a <i><a href="/wiki/Da%27i_al-Mutlaq" title="Da'i al-Mutlaq">Da'i al-Mutlaq</a></i> ("Unrestricted Missionary") maintains contact with him. <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">SunnÄ« Muslims</a> believe that the future Mahdi has not yet arrived on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id=".E1.B8.A4ad.C4.ABth_tradition"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="á¸¤adÄ«th_tradition"><i>á¸¤adÄ«th</i> tradition</span></h4>
<p>ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims believe that the status of Ê¿AlÄ« is supported by numerous <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">á¸¥adÄ«th</a></i>, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadith_of_the_pond_of_Khumm" title="Hadith of the pond of Khumm">Hadith of the pond of Khumm</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadith_of_the_two_weighty_things" title="Hadith of the two weighty things">Hadith of the two weighty things</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadith_of_the_pen_and_paper" title="Hadith of the pen and paper">Hadith of the pen and paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadith_of_warning" title="Hadith of warning">Hadith of the invitation of the close families</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hadith_of_the_Twelve_Successors" title="Hadith of the Twelve Successors">Hadith of the Twelve Successors</a>. In particular, the <a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Kisa" title="Ahl al-Kisa">Hadith of the Cloak</a> is often quoted to illustrate Muhammad's feeling towards Ê¿AlÄ« and his family by both SunnÄ« and ShÄ«Ê¿a scholars. ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims prefer to study and read the <i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> attributed to the <i><a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">Ahl al-Bayt</a></i> and close associates, and most <a href="/wiki/List_of_Shia_books#Hadith_collections" title="List of Shia books">have their own separate <i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> canon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Holy_Relics_.28Tabarruk.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_Relics_(Tabarruk)">Holy Relics (<i>Tabarruk</i>)</span></h4>
<p>It is believed that the armaments and sacred items of all of the prophets, including <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, were handed down in succession to the Imams of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">Ahl al-Bayt</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Ja%27far_al-Sadiq" title="Ja'far al-Sadiq">JaÊ¿far al-á¹¢Ädiq</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">6th ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te Imam</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Kitab_al-Kafi" title="Kitab al-Kafi">Kitab al-Kafi</a></i> mentions that "with me are the arms of the Messenger of Allah. It is not disputable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulayni_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kulayni-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further, he claims that with him is the sword of the Messenger of God, his coat of arms, his Lamam (pennon) and his helmet. In addition, he mentions that with him is the flag of the Messenger of God, the victorious. With him is the Staff of <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, the ring of <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">Solomon</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/David" title="David">David</a>, and the tray on which Moses used to offer his offerings. With him is the name that whenever the Messenger of God would place it between the Muslims and pagans no arrow from the pagans would reach the Muslims. With him is the similar object that angels brought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulayni_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kulayni-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Al-á¹¢Ädiq also narrated that the passing down of armaments is synonymous to receiving the <i>Imamat</i> (leadership), similar to how the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ark_of_Covenant" title="Ark of Covenant">Ark of Covenant</a> in the house of the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> signaled prophethood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulayni_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kulayni-44">[44]</a></sup> Imam <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ali_al-Ridha" title="Ali al-Ridha">Ali al-Ridha</a> narrates that wherever the armaments among us would go, knowledge would also follow and the armaments would never depart from those with knowledge (<i>Imamat</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kulayni_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kulayni-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_doctrines">Other doctrines</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrine_about_necessity_of_acquiring_knowledge">Doctrine about necessity of acquiring knowledge</span></h4>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Rida_al-Muzaffar" title="Muhammad Rida al-Muzaffar">Muhammad Rida al-Muzaffar</a>, God gives humans the faculty of reason and argument. Also, God orders humans to spend time thinking carefully on creation while he refers to all creations as his signs of power and glory. These signs encompass all of the universe. Furthermore, there is a similarity between humans as the little world and the universe as the large world. God does not accept the faith of those who follow him without thinking and only with imitation, but also God blames them for such actions. In other words, humans have to think about the universe with reason and intellect, a faculty bestowed on us by God. Since there is more insistence on the faculty of intellect among ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims, even evaluating the claims of someone who claims prophecy is on the basis of intellect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrine_concerning_prayer">Doctrine concerning prayer</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Salah" title="Salah">Salah</a></div>
<p>Praying in ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam has an important place, as Muhammad described it as a weapon of the <a href="/wiki/Mumin" title="Mumin">believer</a>. In fact, DuÊ¼a considered as something that is a feature of Shia community in a sense. Performing DuÊ¼a in ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam has a special ritual. Because of this, there are many books written on the instructions and conditions of praying among ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims. <a href="/wiki/Shia_clergy" title="Shia clergy">ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te clergymen</a> always invited their followers to recite DuÊ¼a. For instance, Ê¿AlÄ« has been considered with the subject of DuÊ¼a because of his leadership in monotheism.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices">Practices</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karbala_in_2019.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Karbala_in_2019.jpg/250px-Karbala_in_2019.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Karbala_in_2019.jpg/375px-Karbala_in_2019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Karbala_in_2019.jpg/500px-Karbala_in_2019.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karbala_in_2019.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims gathered in <a href="/wiki/Salah" title="Salah">prayer</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Imam_Husayn_Shrine" title="Imam Husayn Shrine">Shrine of Imam á¸¤usayn</a> in <a href="/wiki/Karbala" title="Karbala">Karbala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a></div></div></div>
<p>ShÄ«Ê¿a religious practices, such as prayers, differ only slightly from the SunnÄ«s. While all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">Muslims pray</a> five times daily, ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims have the option of combining <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhuhr" title="Dhuhr">Dhuhr</a></i> with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asr" title="Asr">Asr</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maghrib" title="Maghrib">Maghrib</a></i> with <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isha%27" title="Isha'">Isha'</a></i>, as there are three distinct times mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>. The SunnÄ«s tend to combine only under certain circumstances.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Holidays">Holidays</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shia_days_of_remembrance" title="Shia days of remembrance">Shia days of remembrance</a></div>
<p>ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims celebrate the following annual holidays:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eid_ul-Fitr" title="Eid ul-Fitr">Eid ul-Fitr</a>, which marks the end of fasting during the month of <a href="/wiki/Ramadan_(calendar_month)" title="Ramadan (calendar month)">Ramadan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eid_al-Adha" title="Eid al-Adha">Eid al-Adha</a>, which marks the end of the <i><a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a></i> or pilgrimage to Mecca</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eid_al-Ghadeer" title="Eid al-Ghadeer">Eid al-Ghadeer</a>, which is the anniversary of the Ghadir Khum, the occasion when Muhammad announced Ali's Imamate before a multitude of Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Eid al-Ghadeer is held on the 18th of Dhu al-Hijjah.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Mourning_of_Muharram" title="Mourning of Muharram">Mourning of Muharram</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_of_Ashura" title="Day of Ashura">Day of Ashura</a> for ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims commemorate the martyrdom of <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a>, brother of <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">á¸¤asan</a> and grandson of Muhammad, who was killed by Yazid ibn Muawiyah in <a href="/wiki/Karbala" title="Karbala">Karbala</a> (central Iraq). Ashura is a day of deep mourning which occurs on the 10th of <a href="/wiki/Muharram" title="Muharram">Muharram</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arba%27een" title="Arba'een">Arba'een</a> commemorates the suffering of the women and children of á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ«'s household. After á¸¤usayn was killed, they were marched over the desert, from Karbala (central Iraq) to Shaam (<a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, Syria). Many children (some of whom were direct descendants of Muhammad) died of thirst and exposure along the route. Arbaein occurs on the 20th of <a href="/wiki/Safar" title="Safar">Safar</a>, 40 days after Ashura.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mawlid" title="Mawlid">Mawlid</a>, Muhammad's birth date. Unlike SunnÄ« Muslims, who celebrate the 12th of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rabi%27_al-awwal" title="Rabi' al-awwal">Rabi' al-awwal</a> as Muhammad's day of birth or death (because they assert that his birth and death both occur in this week), ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims celebrate Muhammad's birthday on the 17th of the month, which coincides with the birth date of <a href="/wiki/Ja%27far_al-Sadiq" title="Ja'far al-Sadiq">JaÊ¿far al-á¹¢Ädiq</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">6th ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te Imam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimah" title="Fatimah">FÄá¹­imah</a>'s birthday on 20th of <a href="/wiki/Jumada_al-Thani" title="Jumada al-Thani">Jumada al-Thani</a>. This day is also considered as the "'women and mothers' day"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ«</a>'s birthday on 13th of <a href="/wiki/Rajab" title="Rajab">Rajab</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mid-Sha%27ban" title="Mid-Sha'ban">Mid-Sha'ban</a> is the birth date of the 12th and final Twelver imam, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad al-Mahdi">Muhammad al-Mahdi</a>. It is celebrated by Shia Muslims on the 15th of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sha%27aban" title="Sha'aban">Sha'aban</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laylat_al-Qadr" title="Laylat al-Qadr">Laylat al-Qadr</a>, anniversary of the night of the revelation of the Quran.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eid_al-Mubahila" title="Eid al-Mubahila">Eid al-Mubahila</a> celebrates a meeting between the <i><a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">Ahl al-Bayt</a></i> (household of Muhammad) and a Christian deputation from Najran. Al-Mubahila is held on the 24th of Dhu al-Hijjah.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Holy_sites">Holy sites</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Holiest_sites_in_Shia_Islam" title="Holiest sites in Shia Islam">Holiest sites in Shia Islam</a></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Holiest_sites_in_Islam" title="Holiest sites in Islam">four holy cities of Islam</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>), the cities of <a href="/wiki/Najaf" title="Najaf">Najaf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karbala" title="Karbala">Karbala</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Qom" title="Qom">Qom</a> are the most revered by ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sardeg_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sardeg-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Imam_Ali_Shrine" title="Imam Ali Shrine">Sanctuary of ImÄm Ê¿AlÄ«</a> in Najaf, the <a href="/wiki/Imam_Husayn_Shrine" title="Imam Husayn Shrine">Shrine of Imam á¸¤usayn</a> in Karbala, and the <a href="/wiki/Fatima_Masumeh_Shrine" title="Fatima Masumeh Shrine">Shrine of FÄá¹­imah al-MaÊ¿á¹£Å«mah</a> in Qom are very essential for ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims. Other venerated pilgrimage sites include the <a href="/wiki/Imam_Reza_shrine" title="Imam Reza shrine">Imam Reza shrine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mashhad" title="Mashhad">Mashhad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Al-Kadhimiya_Mosque" title="Al-Kadhimiya Mosque">Kadhimiya Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kadhimiya" title="Kadhimiya">Kadhimiya</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Askari_Mosque" title="Al-Askari Mosque">Al-Askari Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Samarra" title="Samarra">Samarra</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahla_Mosque" title="Sahla Mosque">Sahla Mosque</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kufa" title="Great Mosque of Kufa">Great Mosque of Kufa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jamkaran_Mosque" title="Jamkaran Mosque">Jamkaran Mosque</a> in Qom, and the <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Daniel" title="Tomb of Daniel">Tomb of Daniel</a> in <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>.
</p><p>Most of the <a href="/wiki/Destruction_of_early_Islamic_heritage_sites_in_Saudi_Arabia" title="Destruction of early Islamic heritage sites in Saudi Arabia">ShÄ«Ê¿a sacred places and heritage sites in Saudi Arabia have been destroyed</a> by the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saud" title="House of Saud">Al Saud</a>-<a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabi</a> armies of the <a href="/wiki/Ikhwan" title="Ikhwan">Ikhwan</a>, the most notable being the tombs of the Imams located in the Al-Baqi' cemetery in 1925.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In 2006, a bomb destroyed the shrine of Al-Askari Mosque.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> (<i>See</i>: <a href="/wiki/Anti-Shi%27ism" title="Anti-Shi'ism">Anti-Shi'ism</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">Islam by country</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Islam_by_country.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="922" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Islam_by_country.png/400px-Islam_by_country.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Islam_by_country.png/600px-Islam_by_country.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Islam_by_country.png/800px-Islam_by_country.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Islam_by_country.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Islam by country <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#4a6600; color:white;">Â </span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#a8e600; color:black;">Â </span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#f8ffe6; color:black;">Â </span> <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">SunnÄ«</a> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#66004a; color:white;">Â </span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#cc0096; color:white;">Â </span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#ffe6f8; color:black;">Â </span> ShÄ«Ê¿a <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#000000; color:white;">Â </span> <a href="/wiki/Ibadi_Islam" title="Ibadi Islam">Ibadi</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Madhhab_Map3.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3882" data-file-width="8000" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Madhhab_Map3.png/400px-Madhhab_Map3.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Madhhab_Map3.png/600px-Madhhab_Map3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Madhhab_Map3.png/800px-Madhhab_Map3.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Madhhab_Map3.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Muslim world's schools of jurisprudence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>It is estimated that either 10â20%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-55">[55]</a></sup> or 10â13%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaShiite1_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaShiite1-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRC_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-PRC-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mgmpPRC_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-mgmpPRC-58">[58]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">global Muslim population</a> are ShÄ«Ê¿as. They may number up to 200Â million as of 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRC_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-PRC-57">[57]</a></sup> As of 1985, ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims are estimated to be 21% of the Muslim population in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, although the total number is difficult to estimate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims form a majority of the population in various regions of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Azerbaijan" title="Islam in Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Bahrain" title="Islam in Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Iran" title="Islam in Iran">Iran</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Iraq" title="Islam in Iraq">Iraq</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mafhoum1_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-mafhoum1-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> as well as a plurality in <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Lebanon" title="Islam in Lebanon">Lebanon</a>. ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims constitute 36.3% of the entire population (and 38.6% of the Muslim population) of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sha_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-sha-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates have placed the proportion of ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims in Lebanon between 27% and 45% of the population,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mafhoum1_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-mafhoum1-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> 30%â35% of the citizen population in <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Kuwait" title="Islam in Kuwait">Kuwait</a> (no figures exist for the non-citizen population),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-irfr2012_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-irfr2012-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssi_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssi-65">[65]</a></sup> over 20% in <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Turkey" title="Islam in Turkey">Turkey</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRC_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-PRC-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> 5â20% of the population in <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Pakistan" title="Islam in Pakistan">Pakistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRC_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-PRC-57">[57]</a></sup> and 10â19% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%27a_Islam_in_Afghanistan" title="Shi'a Islam in Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>'s population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lcweb2.loc.gov_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-lcweb2.loc.gov-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAAFG_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIAAFG-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> hosts a number of distinct ShÄ«Ê¿a communities, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver</a> <a href="/wiki/Bahrani_people" title="Bahrani people">Baharna</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Saudi_Arabia" title="Eastern Province, Saudi Arabia">Eastern Province</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nakhawila" title="Nakhawila">Nakhawila</a> of Medina, and the <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«</a> <a href="/wiki/Sulaymani" title="Sulaymani">Sulaymani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zaydism" title="Zaydism">ZaydÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿as</a> of <a href="/wiki/Najran" title="Najran">Najran</a>. Estimations put the number of ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te citizens at 2â4Â million,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> accounting for roughly 15% of the local population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Approximately 40% of the population of Yemen are ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Significant ShÄ«Ê¿a communities also exist in the coastal regions of <a href="/wiki/West_Sumatra" title="West Sumatra">West Sumatra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">Aceh</a> in Indonesia (see <a href="/wiki/Tabuik" title="Tabuik">Tabuik</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leo_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leo-73">[73]</a></sup> The ShÄ«Ê¿a presence is negligible elsewhere in Southeast Asia, where Muslims are predominantly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shafi%CA%BDi_school" title="ShafiÊ½i school">ShÄfiÊ¿Ä«</a> SunnÄ«s.
</p><p>A significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%27a_Islam_in_Nigeria" title="Shi'a Islam in Nigeria">ShÄ«Ê¿a minority is present in Nigeria</a>, made up of modern-era converts to a <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Movement_(Nigeria)" title="Islamic Movement (Nigeria)">ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te movement</a> centered around <a href="/wiki/Kano_State" title="Kano State">Kano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_State" title="Sokoto State">Sokoto</a> states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRC_57-4"><a href="#cite_note-PRC-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mgmpPRC_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-mgmpPRC-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Several African countries like <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nairobi_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nairobi-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MatthÃ©e2008_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-MatthÃ©e2008-76">[76]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> etc. hold small minority populations of various ShÄ«Ê¿a subsects, primarily descendants of immigrants from South Asia during the colonial period, such as the <a href="/wiki/Khoja" title="Khoja">Khoja</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaseMiyake2002_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-HaseMiyake2002-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
