<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_denominations_or_branches">Major denominations or branches</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches#ShÄ«Ê¿a_Islam" title="Islamic schools and branches">Islamic schools and branches Â§Â ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_extinct_Shia_sects" title="List of extinct Shia sects">List of extinct Shia sects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Schools_of_Islamic_theology#ShÄ«Ê¿a_schools_of_theology" title="Schools of Islamic theology">Schools of Islamic theology Â§Â ShÄ«Ê¿a schools of theology</a></div>
<p>The ShÄ«Ê¿a community throughout its history split over the issue of the Imamate. The largest branch are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelvers" title="Twelvers">Twelvers</a>, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Zaydism" title="Zaydism">ZaydÄ«s</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s</a>. Each subsect of ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm follows its own line of Imamate. All mainstream Twelver and IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims follow the same school of thought, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ja%CA%BDfari_jurisprudence" title="JaÊ½fari jurisprudence">JaÊ½fari jurisprudence</a>, named after <a href="/wiki/Ja%27far_al-Sadiq" title="Ja'far al-Sadiq">JaÊ¿far al-á¹¢Ädiq</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">6th ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te Imam</a>. <a href="/wiki/Shia_clergy" title="Shia clergy">ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te clergymen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Faq%C4%ABh" title="FaqÄ«h">jurists</a> usually carry the title of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mujtahid" title="Mujtahid">mujtahid</a></i> (i.e., someone authorized to issue legal opinions in ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Twelver">Twelver</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver</a></div>
<p><b>Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm</b> or <b>IthnÄÊ¿ashariyyah</b> is the largest branch of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam, and the terms <i>ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslim</i> and <i>ShÄ«Ê¿a</i> often refer to the Twelvers by default. The designation <i>Twelver</i> is derived from the doctrine of believing in twelve divinely ordained leaders, known as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">the Twelve Imams</a>". Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿as are otherwise known as <i>Imami</i> or <i>JaÊ¿fari</i>; the latter term derives from <a href="/wiki/Ja%27far_al-Sadiq" title="Ja'far al-Sadiq">JaÊ¿far al-á¹¢Ädiq</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">6th ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te Imam</a>, who elaborated the Twelver jurisprudence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿as constitute the majority of the population in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (90%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> (85%),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica738_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica738-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a> (70%), <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> (65%), and <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> (65% of Muslims).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esp45_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-esp45-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glob_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-glob-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-review_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-review-96">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrine">Doctrine</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Names_of_imameen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="433" decoding="async" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Names_of_imameen.jpg/300px-Names_of_imameen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/Names_of_imameen.jpg 1.5x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Names_of_imameen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Names of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">the 12 Imams</a> (descendants of Imam Ê¿AlÄ«) written in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">calligraphic form</a> of the name <i>Ê¿AlÄ«</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">Ø¹ÙÙ</span></div></div></div>
<p>Twelver doctrine is based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theology_of_Twelvers" title="Theology of Twelvers">five principles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> These five principles known as <i>Usul ad-Din</i> are as follow:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rich2006_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rich2006-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><b><a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">Monotheism</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> is one and unique;</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Adl" title="Adl">Justice</a></b>: the concept of moral rightness based on ethics, fairness, and equity, along with the punishment of the breach of these ethics;</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nubuwwah" title="Nubuwwah">Prophethood</a></b>: the institution by which God sends emissaries, or prophets, to guide humankind;</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Shia_doctrine" title="Imamate in Shia doctrine">Leadership</a></b>: a divine institution which succeeded the institution of Prophethood. Its appointees (<i>Imams</i>) are divinely appointed;</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_of_Resurrection" title="Day of Resurrection">Resurrection and Last Judgment</a></b>: God's final assessment of humanity.</li></ol>
<p>More specifically, these principles are known as <i>Usul al-Madhhab</i> (principles of the ShÄ«Ê¿a branch of Islam) according to Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿as, which differ from <i>Daruriyat al-Din</i> ("Necessities of Religion"), which are principles in order for one to be a Muslim. <i>Daruriyat al-Din</i> don't include leadership (<i>Imamah</i>), as it is not a requirement in order for one to be recognized as a Muslim. However, this category, according to Twelver scholars like <a href="/wiki/Ayatollah" title="Ayatollah">Ayatollah</a> <a href="/wiki/Abu_al-Qasim_al-Khoei" title="Abu al-Qasim al-Khoei">Abu al-Qasim al-Khoei</a>, does include belief in God, prophethood, the Day of Resurrection, and other "necessities" (such as the <a href="/wiki/Angels_in_Islam" title="Angels in Islam">belief in angels</a>). In this regard, Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿as draw a distinction in terms of believing in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_pillars_of_Islam" title="Five pillars of Islam">main principles of Islam</a> on the one hand, and specifically ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te doctrines like the <a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Shia_doctrine" title="Imamate in Shia doctrine">Imamate</a> on the other.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Books">Books</span></h4>
<p>Besides the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, which is the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_holy_books" title="Islamic holy books">sacred text</a> common to all Muslims, Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿as derive scriptural and authoritative guidance from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_hadith_Books" title="List of hadith Books">collections of sayings and traditions</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith"><i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i></a>) attributed to Muhammad and the Twelve Imams. Below is a list of some of the most prominent of these books:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Nahj_al-Balagha" title="Nahj al-Balagha">Nahj al-Balagha</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Al-Sharif_al-Radi" title="Al-Sharif al-Radi">Ash-Sharif Ar-Radhi</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> â the most famous collection of sermons, letters &amp; narration attributed to Ali, the first Imam regarded by Shias</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kitab_al-Kafi" title="Kitab al-Kafi">Kitab al-Kafi</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Ya%27qub_al-Kulayni" title="Muhammad ibn Ya'qub al-Kulayni">Muhammad ibn Ya'qub al-Kulayni</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Wasa%27il_al-Shia" title="Wasa'il al-Shia">Wasa'il al-ShiÊ»ah</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Al-Hurr_al-Amili" title="Al-Hurr al-Amili">al-Hurr al-Amili</a></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Twelve_Imams">The Twelve Imams</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Twelver_doctrine" title="Imamate in Twelver doctrine">Imamate in Twelver doctrine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">Sunni reports about there being 12 successors to the Prophet</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hadith_of_the_Twelve_Successors" title="Hadith of the Twelve Successors">Hadith of the Twelve Successors</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Occultation_(Islam)" title="Occultation (Islam)">Occultation (Islam)</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reappearance_of_Hujjat_Allah_al-Mahdi" title="Reappearance of Hujjat Allah al-Mahdi">Reappearance of Hujjat Allah al-Mahdi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Fourteen_Infallibles" title="The Fourteen Infallibles">The Fourteen Infallibles</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Twelvers1_copy_copy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3246" data-file-width="3250" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Twelvers1_copy_copy.png/240px-Twelvers1_copy_copy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Twelvers1_copy_copy.png/360px-Twelvers1_copy_copy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Twelvers1_copy_copy.png/480px-Twelvers1_copy_copy.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Twelvers1_copy_copy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Calligraphic representation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">the 12 Imams</a> along with the name of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_prophet" title="Islamic prophet">Islamic prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Twelve Imams</i> are the <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">spiritual and political successors to Muhammad</a> for the Twelvers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> According to the theology of Twelvers, the successor of Muhammad is an <a href="/wiki/Ismah" title="Ismah">infallible</a> human individual who not only rules over the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a> with justice but also is able to keep and interpret the <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">divine law</a> (<i>sharÄ«Ê¿a</i>) and its esoteric meaning. The words and deeds of Muhammad and the Twelve Imams are a guide and model for the Muslim community to follow; as a result, they must be free from error and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_sin" title="Islamic views on sin">sin</a>, and Imams must be chosen by <a href="/wiki/Nass_(Islam)" title="Nass (Islam)">divine decree</a> (<i>nass</i>) through Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nasr_a_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nasr_a-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Momen_1985,_p._174_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Momen_1985,_p._174-102">[101]</a></sup> In Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm, each Imam was the son of the previous Imam, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a>, who was the brother of <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">á¸¤asan ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The twelfth and final Imam is <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad al-Mahdi">Hujjat Allah al-Mahdi</a>, who is believed by Twelvers to be currently alive and hidden in <a href="/wiki/Occultation_(Islam)" title="Occultation (Islam)">Occultation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Imamat_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Imamat-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jurisprudence">Jurisprudence</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ja%27fari_jurisprudence" title="Ja'fari jurisprudence">Ja'fari jurisprudence</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Shia_clergy" title="Shia clergy">Shia clergy</a></div>
<p>The Twelver jurisprudence is called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ja%CA%BDfari_jurisprudence" title="JaÊ½fari jurisprudence">JaÊ½fari jurisprudence</a></i>. In this <a href="/wiki/Madhhab#Shia" title="Madhhab">school of Islamic jurisprudence</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i> is considered to be comprehensive of the oral traditions of Muhammad and their implementation and interpretation by the Twelve Imams. There are three schools of JaÊ¿fari jurisprudence: Usuli, Akhbari, and <a href="/wiki/Shaykhism" title="Shaykhism">Shaykhi</a>; the Usuli school is by far the largest of the three. Twelver groups that don't follow the JaÊ¿fari jurisprudence include <a href="/wiki/Alevism" title="Alevism">Alevis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bektashi_Order" title="Bektashi Order">Bektashi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Qizilbash" title="Qizilbash">Qizilbash</a>.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_pillars_of_Islam" title="Five pillars of Islam">five pillars of Islam</a> to the JaÊ¿fari jurisprudence are known as <i>Usul ad-Din</i>:
</p>
<ol><li><i><a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">Tawá¸¥Ä«d</a></i>: unity and oneness of God;</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">Nubuwwah</a></i>: prophethood of Muhammad;</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology#Resurrection_and_final_judgement" title="Islamic eschatology">MuÊ¿ad</a></i>: resurrection and final judgment;</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Adl" title="Adl">Ê¿Adl</a></i>: justice of God;</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Shia_doctrine" title="Imamate in Shia doctrine">Imamah</a></i>: the rightful place of the ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te Imams.</li></ol>
<p>In JaÊ¿fari jurisprudence, there are eight secondary pillars, known as <i>Furu ad-Din</i>, which are as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rich2006_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rich2006-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">Salat</a></i> (prayer);</li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sawm" title="Sawm">Sawm</a></i> (fasting);</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a></i> (pilgrimage) to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>;</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">ZakÄt</a></i> (alms giving to the poor);</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">JihÄd</a></i> (struggle) for the righteous cause;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ma%27ruf" title="Ma'ruf">Directing others towards good</a>;</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forbidding_what_is_evil" title="Forbidding what is evil">Directing others away from evil</a>;</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Khums" title="Khums">Khums</a></i> (20% tax on savings yearly, after deduction of commercial expenses).</li></ol>
<p>According to Twelvers, defining and interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Islamic jurisprudence (<i>fiqh</i>)</a> is the responsibility of Muhammad and the Twelve Imams. Since the <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad al-Mahdi">12th Imam</a> is currently in <a href="/wiki/Occultation_(Islam)" title="Occultation (Islam)">Occultation</a>, it is the duty of <a href="/wiki/Shia_clergy" title="Shia clergy">ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te clerics</a> to refer to the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_literature" title="Islamic literature">Islamic literature</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith"><i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i></a>, and identify legal decisions within the confines of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a> to provide means to deal with current issues from an Islamic perspective. In other words, clergymen in Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm are believed to be the guardians of <i>fiqh</i>, which is believed to have been defined by Muhammad and his twelve successors. This process is known as <i><a href="/wiki/Ijtihad" title="Ijtihad">ijtihad</a></i> and the clerics are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Marja%27" title="Marja'">marjaÊ¿</a></i>, meaning "reference"; the labels <i><a href="/wiki/Allamah" title="Allamah">Allamah</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ayatollah" title="Ayatollah">Ayatollah</a></i> are in use for Twelver clerics.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamists">Islamists</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islamist_Shi%27ism" title="Islamist Shi'ism">Islamist ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm</a> (<a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">ØªØ´ÛØ¹ Ø§Ø®ÙØ§ÙÛ</span>) is a new denomination within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver_Shi%E2%80%99ism" title="Twelver Shiâism">Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm</a> greatly inspired by the political ideology of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a> and mysticism of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Arabi" title="Ibn Arabi">Ibn Arabi</a>. It sees Islam as a political system and differs from the other mainstream <a href="/wiki/Usuli" title="Usuli">Usuli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Akhbari" title="Akhbari">Akhbari</a> groups in favoring the idea of the establishment of an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_state" title="Islamic state">Islamic state</a> in <a href="/wiki/Occultation_(Islam)" title="Occultation (Islam)">Occultation</a> under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad al-Mahdi">12th Imam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhalaji200964_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhalaji200964-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBohdan2020243_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBohdan2020243-105">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hadi_Khosroshahi" title="Hadi Khosroshahi">Hadi Khosroshahi</a> was the first person to identify himself as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ikhwan_al-Muslimeen" title="Ikhwan al-Muslimeen">ikhwani</a></i> (Islamist) ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> Because of the concept of the hidden Imam, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad al-Mahdi">Muhammad al-Mahdi</a>, ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam is inherently secular in the age of Occultation, therefore Islamist ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims had to borrow ideas from SunnÄ« Islamists and adjust them in accordance with the doctrine of ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBohdan2020250â251_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBohdan2020250â251-107">[106]</a></sup> Its foundations were laid during the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Constitutional_Revolution" title="Persian Constitutional Revolution">Persian Constitutional Revolution</a> at the start of 20th century in <a href="/wiki/Qajar_Iran" title="Qajar Iran">Qajar Iran</a> (1905â1911), when <a href="/wiki/Fazlullah_Nouri" title="Fazlullah Nouri">Fazlullah Nouri</a> supported the <a href="/wiki/List_of_monarchs_of_Persia" title="List of monarchs of Persia">Persian king</a> <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Qajar" title="Ahmad Shah Qajar">Ahmad Shah Qajar</a> against the will of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Kazim_Khurasani" title="Muhammad Kazim Khurasani">Muhammad Kazim Khurasani</a>, the Usuli <i><a href="/wiki/Marja%27" title="Marja'">marjaÊ¿</a></i> of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Ism.C4.81.CA.BF.C4.ABl.C4.AB_.28Sevener.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«_(Sevener)">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« (<i>Sevener</i>)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">Isma'ilism</a></div>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s</a></b>, otherwise known as <i>Sevener</i>, derive their name from their acceptance of <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_ibn_Ja%27far" title="Isma'il ibn Ja'far">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«l ibn JaÊ¿far</a> as the divinely appointed spiritual successor (<a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Ismaili_doctrine" title="Imamate in Ismaili doctrine">Imam</a>) to <a href="/wiki/Ja%27far_al-Sadiq" title="Ja'far al-Sadiq">JaÊ¿far al-á¹¢Ädiq</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Imams" title="The Twelve Imams">6th ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te Imam</a>, wherein they differ from the Twelvers, who recognize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musa_al-Kadhim" title="Musa al-Kadhim">MÅ«sÄ al-KÄáºim</a>, younger brother of IsmÄÊ¿Ä«l, as the true Imam.
</p><p>After the death or Occultation of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Isma%27il" title="Muhammad ibn Isma'il">Muhammad ibn Imam IsmÄÊ¿Ä«l</a> in the 8th century CE, the teachings of IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«sm further transformed into the belief system as it is known today, with an explicit concentration on the deeper, esoteric meaning (<i><a href="/wiki/Batin_(Islam)" title="Batin (Islam)">bÄá¹­in</a></i>) of the Islamic faith. With the eventual development of Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm into the more literalistic <i>(<a href="/wiki/Zahir_(Islam)" title="Zahir (Islam)">zahÄ«r</a>)</i> oriented Akhbari and later Usuli schools of thought, ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm further developed in two separate directions: the metaphorical IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« group focusing on the <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">mystical</a> path and nature of God and the divine manifestation in the personage of the "Imam of the Time" as the "Face of God", with the more literalistic Twelver group focusing on <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">divine law</a> (<i>sharÄ«'ah</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">deeds and sayings</a> (<i>sunnah</i>) attributed to Muhammad and <a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">his successors</a> (the <i>Ahl al-Bayt</i>), who as A'immah were guides and a <a href="/wiki/N%C5%ABr_(Islam)" title="NÅ«r (Islam)">light</a> (<i>nÅ«r</i>) to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:His_Highness_the_Aga_Khan_(15760993697).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2792" data-file-width="2576" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/His_Highness_the_Aga_Khan_%2815760993697%29.jpg/230px-His_Highness_the_Aga_Khan_%2815760993697%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/His_Highness_the_Aga_Khan_%2815760993697%29.jpg/345px-His_Highness_the_Aga_Khan_%2815760993697%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/His_Highness_the_Aga_Khan_%2815760993697%29.jpg/460px-His_Highness_the_Aga_Khan_%2815760993697%29.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:His_Highness_the_Aga_Khan_(15760993697).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">ShÄh</a> Karim al-Husayni, known as the <a href="/wiki/Aga_Khan_IV" title="Aga Khan IV">Aga Khan IV</a>, is the 49th and current Imam of <a href="/wiki/Nizari_Isma%27ilism" title="Nizari Isma'ilism">NizÄrÄ« IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Though there are several subsects amongst the IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s, the term in today's vernacular generally refers to the ShÄ«Ê¿a Imami IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« <a href="/wiki/Nizari_Isma%27ilism" title="Nizari Isma'ilism">NizÄrÄ« community</a>, often referred to as the <i>IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s</i> by default, who are followers of the <a href="/wiki/Aga_Khan" title="Aga Khan">Aga Khan</a> and the largest group within IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«sm. Another ShÄ«Ê¿a Imami IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« community are the <a href="/wiki/Dawoodi_Bohra" title="Dawoodi Bohra">Dawudi Bohras</a>, led by a <i><a href="/wiki/Da%27i_al-Mutlaq" title="Da'i al-Mutlaq">Da'i al-Mutlaq</a></i> ("Unrestricted Missionary") as representative of a hidden Imam. While there are many other branches with extremely differing exterior practices, much of the spiritual theology has remained the same since the days of the faith's early Imams. In recent centuries, IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s have largely been an Indo-Iranian community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> but they can also be found in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, and in recent years several IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s have emigrated to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> (primarily in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>), <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DaftaryShort1998p1_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-DaftaryShort1998p1-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Ism.C4.81.CA.BF.C4.ABl.C4.AB_Imams"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«_Imams">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« Imams</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Ismaili_imams" title="List of Ismaili imams">List of Ismaili imams</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Nizari_Isma%27ilism" title="Nizari Isma'ilism">NizÄrÄ«</a> <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«</a> interpretation of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam, the <a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Nizari_doctrine" title="Imamate in Nizari doctrine">Imam</a> is the guide and the intercessor between humans and God, and the individual through whom God is recognized. He is also responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Esoteric_interpretation_of_the_Quran" title="Esoteric interpretation of the Quran">esoteric interpretation of the Quran</a> (<i>taÊ¾wÄ«l</i>). He is the possessor of divine knowledge and therefore the "Prime Teacher". According to the "Epistle of the Right Path", a Persian IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« prose text from the post-<a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_and_conquests" title="Mongol invasions and conquests">Mongol period</a> of IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« history, by an anonymous author, there has been a chain of Imams since the beginning of time, and there will continue to be an Imam present on the Earth until the end of time. The worlds would not exist in perfection without this uninterrupted <a href="/wiki/List_of_Isma%27ili_imams" title="List of Isma'ili imams">chain of Imams</a>. The proof (<i><a href="/wiki/Hujja" title="Hujja">hujja</a></i>) and gate (<i><a href="/wiki/Bab_(Shia_Islam)" title="Bab (Shia Islam)">bÄb</a></i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Nizari_doctrine" title="Imamate in Nizari doctrine">Imam</a> are always aware of his presence and are witness to this uninterrupted chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the death of <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_ibn_Ja%27far" title="Isma'il ibn Ja'far">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«l ibn JaÊ¿far</a>, many IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s believed that one day the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">eschatological figure</a> of Imam <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a>, whom they believed to be <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Isma%27il" title="Muhammad ibn Isma'il">Muhammad ibn Imam IsmÄÊ¿Ä«l</a>, would return and establish an age of justice. One group included the violent <a href="/wiki/Qarmatians" title="Qarmatians">Qarmatians</a>, who had a stronghold in <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>. In contrast, some IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s believed the Imamate <i>did</i> continue, and that the Imams were in Occultation and still communicated and taught their followers through a network of <i><a href="/wiki/Dawah" title="Dawah">Da'i</a></i> ("Missionaries").
</p><p>In 909 CE, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdullah_al-Mahdi_Billah" title="Abdullah al-Mahdi Billah">Abdullah al-Mahdi Billah</a>, a claimant to the IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« Imamate, established the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a>. During this period, three lineages of Imams were formed. The first branch, known today as the <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a>, began with <a href="/wiki/Al-Hakim_bi-Amr_Allah" title="Al-Hakim bi-Amr Allah">Al-á¸¤Äkim bi-Amr AllÄh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timani_2021_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Timani_2021-115">[114]</a></sup> Born in 985 CE, he ascended as ruler at the age of eleven. When in 1021 CE his mule returned without him, soaked in blood, a religious group that was forming in his lifetime broke off from mainstream IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«sm and didn't acknowledge his successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timani_2021_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Timani_2021-115">[114]</a></sup> Later to be known as the Druze, they believe Al-á¸¤Äkim to be God incarnate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poonawala_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poonawala-116">[115]</a></sup> and the prophesied Mahdi on Earth, who would one day return and bring justice to the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> The Druze faith further split from IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«sm as it developed into a distinct <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic religion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethno-religious_group" title="Ethno-religious group">ethno-religious group</a> with its own unique doctrines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timani_2021_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Timani_2021-115">[114]</a></sup> and finally separated from both IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«sm and Islam altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timani_2021_115-3"><a href="#cite_note-Timani_2021-115">[114]</a></sup> Thus, the Druze don't identify themselves as Muslims,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timani_2021_115-4"><a href="#cite_note-Timani_2021-115">[114]</a></sup> and aren't considered as such by Muslims either.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Timani_2021_115-5"><a href="#cite_note-Timani_2021-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The second split occurred between <a href="/wiki/Nizari_Isma%27ilism" title="Nizari Isma'ilism">NizÄrÄ«</a> and <a href="/wiki/Musta%27li_Ismailism" title="Musta'li Ismailism">MustaâlÄ«</a> IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s following the death of Ma'ad al-Mustansir Billah in 1094 CE. His rule was the longest of any caliph in any Islamic empire. Upon his passing away, his sons, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nizar_(Fatimid_Imam)" title="Nizar (Fatimid Imam)">NizÄr</a> (the older) and <a href="/wiki/Al-Musta%27li" title="Al-Musta'li">Al-MustaâlÄ«</a> (the younger), fought for political and spiritual control of the dynasty. NizÄr was defeated and jailed, but according to the NizÄrÄ« tradition his son escaped to <a href="/wiki/Alamut" title="Alamut">Alamut</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« had accepted his claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DaftaryShort1998p106_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-DaftaryShort1998p106-122">[121]</a></sup> From here on, the NizÄrÄ« IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« community has continued with a present, living Imam.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Musta%27li_Ismailism" title="Musta'li Ismailism">MustaâlÄ« IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s</a> split between the <a href="/wiki/Tayyibi_Isma%27ilism" title="Tayyibi Isma'ilism">á¹¬ayyibi</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hafizi_Isma%27ilism" title="Hafizi Isma'ilism">á¸¤Äfiáºi</a>; á¹¬ayyibi IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s, also known as "Bohras", are further divided between <a href="/wiki/Dawoodi_Bohra" title="Dawoodi Bohra">Dawudi Bohras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulaymani" title="Sulaymani">Sulaymani Bohras</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alavi_Bohras" title="Alavi Bohras">Alavi Bohras</a>. The former denomination claims that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/At-Tayyib_Abi_l-Qasim" title="At-Tayyib Abi l-Qasim">At-Tayyib Abi l-Qasim</a>, son of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Amir_bi-Ahkami_l-Lah" title="Al-Amir bi-Ahkami l-Lah">Al-Amir bi-Ahkami l-Lah</a>, and the Imams following him went into a period of anonymity (<i>Dawr-e-Satr</i>) and appointed a <i><a href="/wiki/Da%27i_al-Mutlaq" title="Da'i al-Mutlaq">Da'i al-Mutlaq</a></i> ("Unrestricted Missionary") to guide the community, in a similar manner as the IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s had lived after the death of Muhammad ibn Imam IsmÄÊ¿Ä«l. The latter denomination claims that the ruling Fatimid caliph was the Imam, and they died out with the fall of the Fatimid Empire.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pillars">Pillars</span></h4>
<p>IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s have categorized their practices which are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Seven_pillars_of_Ismailism" title="Seven pillars of Ismailism">seven pillars</a></i>:
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_leadership">Contemporary leadership</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nizari_Isma%27ilism" title="Nizari Isma'ilism">NizÄrÄ«s</a> place importance on a scholarly institution because of the existence of a present Imam. The Imam of the Age defines the jurisprudence, and his guidance may differ with Imams previous to him because of different times and circumstances. For NizÄrÄ« IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s, the current Imam is Karim al-Husayni <a href="/wiki/Aga_Khan_IV" title="Aga Khan IV">Aga Khan IV</a>. The NizÄrÄ« line of Imams has continued to this day as an uninterrupted chain.
</p><p>Divine leadership has continued in the Bohra branch through the institution of the "Missionary" (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Dai_of_Dawoodi_Bohra" title="List of Dai of Dawoodi Bohra">Da'i</a></i>). According to the Bohra tradition, before the last Imam, At-Tayyib Abi l-Qasim, went into seclusion, his father, the 20th Al-Amir bi-Ahkami l-Lah, had instructed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Hurra_Al-Malika" title="Al-Hurra Al-Malika">Al-Hurra Al-Malika</a> the <a href="/wiki/Malik" title="Malik">Malika</a> (<a href="/wiki/Queen_consort" title="Queen consort">Queen consort</a>) in Yemen to appoint a vicegerent after the seclusionâthe <i><a href="/wiki/Da%27i_al-Mutlaq" title="Da'i al-Mutlaq">Da'i al-Mutlaq</a></i> ("Unrestricted Missionary"), who as the Imam's vicegerent has full authority to govern the community in all matters both spiritual and temporal while the lineage of <a href="/wiki/Musta%27li_Ismailism" title="Musta'li Ismailism">MustaâlÄ«</a>-<a href="/wiki/Tayyibi_Isma%27ilism" title="Tayyibi Isma'ilism">á¹¬ayyibi</a> Imams remains in seclusion (<i>Dawr-e-Satr</i>). The three branches of MustaâlÄ« IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s (<a href="/wiki/Dawoodi_Bohra" title="Dawoodi Bohra">Dawudi Bohras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulaymani" title="Sulaymani">Sulaymani Bohras</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alavi_Bohras" title="Alavi Bohras">Alavi Bohras</a>) differ on who the current "Unrestricted Missionary" is.
</p>
<h3><span id="Zayd.C4.AB_.28Fiver.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="ZaydÄ«_(Fiver)">ZaydÄ« (<i>Fiver</i>)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Zaydism" title="Zaydism">Zaydism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dinar_of_al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq,_AH_298.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="389" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Dinar_of_al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq%2C_AH_298.jpg/240px-Dinar_of_al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq%2C_AH_298.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Dinar_of_al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq%2C_AH_298.jpg/360px-Dinar_of_al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq%2C_AH_298.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Dinar_of_al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq%2C_AH_298.jpg/480px-Dinar_of_al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq%2C_AH_298.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dinar_of_al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq,_AH_298.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gold_dinar" title="Gold dinar">Gold dinar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq_Yahya" title="Al-Hadi ila'l-Haqq Yahya">al-á¸¤ÄdÄ« ila'l-á¸¤aqq Yaá¸¥yÄ</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_history_of_Yemen" title="Islamic history of Yemen">first ZaydÄ« Imam of Yemen</a>, minted in 910â911 CE.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zaydi_State_1675.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1660" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Zaydi_State_1675.jpg/240px-Zaydi_State_1675.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Zaydi_State_1675.jpg/360px-Zaydi_State_1675.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Zaydi_State_1675.jpg/480px-Zaydi_State_1675.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zaydi_State_1675.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Zaidi_State" title="Yemeni Zaidi State">ZaydÄ« State of Yemen</a> under the rule of Imam <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil_Isma%27il" title="Al-Mutawakkil Isma'il">Al-Mutawakkil IsmÄÊ¿Ä«l bin al-QÄsim</a> (1644â1676)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Zaydism" title="Zaydism">ZaydÄ«sm</a>, otherwise known as <b>ZaydÄ«yyah</b> or <b>ZaydÄ«</b>, is a branch of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam named after <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Ali" title="Zayd ibn Ali">Zayd ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a>. Followers of the ZaydÄ« school of jurisprudence are called ZaydÄ«s or occasionally <i>Fivers</i>. However, there is also a group called <i>ZaydÄ« WasÄ«tÄ«s</i> who are Twelvers (see below). ZaydÄ«s constitute roughly 42â47% of the <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Yemen" title="Demographics of Yemen">population of Yemen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrine_2">Doctrine</span></h4>
<p>The ZaydÄ«s, Twelvers, and IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s all recognize the same first four Imams; however, the ZaydÄ«s consider <a href="/wiki/Zayd_ibn_Ali" title="Zayd ibn Ali">Zayd ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a> as the 5th Imam. After the time of Zayd ibn Ê¿AlÄ«, the ZaydÄ«s believed that <a href="/wiki/Sayyid" title="Sayyid">any descendant</a> (<i>Sayyid</i>) of <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">á¸¤asan ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a> or <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a> could become the next Imam, after fulfilling certain conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Washington_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Washington-125">[124]</a></sup> Other well-known ZaydÄ« Imams in history were Yahya ibn Zayd, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Nafs_al-Zakiyya" title="Muhammad al-Nafs al-Zakiyya">Muhammad al-Nafs al-Zakiyya</a>, and Ibrahim ibn Abdullah.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Imams_of_Yemen" title="Imams of Yemen">ZaydÄ« doctrine of Imamah</a> doesn't presuppose the infallibility of the Imam, nor the belief that the Imams are supposed to receive divine guidance. Moreover, ZaydÄ«s don't believe that the Imamate must pass from father to son but believe it can be held by any <i><a href="/wiki/Sayyid" title="Sayyid">Sayyid</a></i> descended from either <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">á¸¤asan ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a> or <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a> (as was the case after the death of the former). Historically, ZaydÄ«s held that Zayd ibn Ê¿AlÄ« was the rightful successor of the 4th Imam since he led a <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_Zayd_ibn_Ali" title="Revolt of Zayd ibn Ali">rebellion against the Umayyads in protest of their tyranny and corruption</a>. Muhammad al-Baqir did not engage in political action, and the followers of Zayd ibn Ê¿AlÄ« maintained that a true Imam must fight against corrupt rulers.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jurisprudence_2">Jurisprudence</span></h4>
<p>In matters of <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_proofs_in_Shia_jurisprudence" title="Intellectual proofs in Shia jurisprudence">Islamic jurisprudence</a>, ZaydÄ«s follow the teachings of Zayd ibn Ê¿AlÄ«, which are documented in his book <i>Majmu'l Fiqh</i> (in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span style="font-size: 120%;">ÙØ¬ÙÙØ¹ Ø§ÙÙÙÙÙ</span>). <a href="/wiki/Al-Hadi_ila%27l-Haqq_Yahya" title="Al-Hadi ila'l-Haqq Yahya">Al-á¸¤ÄdÄ« ila'l-á¸¤aqq Yaá¸¥yÄ</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_history_of_Yemen" title="Islamic history of Yemen">first ZaydÄ« Imam and founder of the ZaydÄ« State in Yemen</a>, is regarded as the codifier of ZaydÄ« jurisprudence, and as such most ZaydÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿as today are known as <i>Hadawis</i>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Idrisid_dynasty" title="Idrisid dynasty">Idrisids</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><span style="font-size: 120%;">Ø§ÙØ£Ø¯Ø§Ø±Ø³Ø©</span></span>) were Arab<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zaydism" title="Zaydism">ZaydÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿as</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> whose dynasty, named after its first sultan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idris_I" title="Idris I">Idris I</a>, ruled in the western <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> from 788 to 985 CE. Another ZaydÄ« State was established in the region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilan_Province" title="Gilan Province">Gilan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deylaman" title="Deylaman">Deylaman</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tabaristan" title="Tabaristan">Tabaristan</a> (northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>) in 864 CE by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alavids" title="Alavids">Alavids</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> it lasted until the death of its leader at the hand of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samanids" title="Samanids">Samanids</a> in 928 CE. Roughly forty years later, the ZaydÄ« State was revived in Gilan and survived under Hasanid leaders until 1126 CE. Afterwards, from the 12th to 13th centuries, the ZaydÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿as of Deylaman, Gilan, and Tabaristan then acknowledged the ZaydÄ« <a href="/wiki/Imams_of_Yemen" title="Imams of Yemen">Imams of Yemen</a> or rival ZaydÄ« Imams within Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Buyid_dynasty" title="Buyid dynasty">Buyids</a> were initially ZaydÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿as,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup> as were the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Ukhaidhir" title="Banu Ukhaidhir">Banu Ukhaidhir</a> rulers of <a href="/wiki/Al-Yamama" title="Al-Yamama">al-Yamama</a> in the 9th and 10th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> The leader of the ZaydÄ« community took the title of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a>; thus, the ruler of Yemen was known by this title. Al-Hadi Yahya bin al-Hussain bin al-Qasim ar-Rassi, a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">á¸¤asan ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a>, founded the ZaydÄ« Imamate at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sa%27dah" title="Sa'dah">Sa'dah</a> in 893â897 CE, and the <a href="/wiki/Rassid_dynasty" title="Rassid dynasty">Rassid dynasty</a> continued to rule over Yemen until the middle of the 20th century, when the <a href="/wiki/North_Yemen_Civil_War" title="North Yemen Civil War">republican revolution of 1962</a> deposed the last ZaydÄ« Imam. (<i>See</i>: <a href="/wiki/Arab_Cold_War" title="Arab Cold War">Arab Cold War</a>). The founding ZaydÄ« branch in Yemen was the Jarudiyya; however, with increasing interaction with the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">á¸¤anafÄ«</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shafi%CA%BDi_school" title="ShafiÊ½i school">ShÄfiÊ¿Ä«</a> schools of <a href="/wiki/Madhhab#Sunni" title="Madhhab">SunnÄ« jurisprudence</a>, there was a shift from the Jarudiyya group to the Sulaimaniyya, Tabiriyya, Butriyya, and Salihiyya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> ZaydÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿as form the <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Yemen" title="Religion in Yemen">second dominant religious group in Yemen</a>. Currently, they constitute about 40â45% of the population in Yemen; JaÊ¿faris and IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s constitute the 2â5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, it is estimated that there are over 1Â million ZaydÄ« ShÄ«Ê¿as, primarily based in the western provinces.
</p><p>Currently, the most prominent ZaydÄ« political movement is the <a href="/wiki/Houthi_movement" title="Houthi movement">Houthi movement</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nevola-Shiban_2020_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nevola-Shiban_2020-139">[138]</a></sup> known by the name of <i>Shabab al-Mu'mineen</i> ("Believing Youth") or <i>Ansar Allah</i> ("Partisans of God").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glenn_2015_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn_2015-140">[139]</a></sup> In 2014â2015, <a href="/wiki/Houthi_takeover_in_Yemen" title="Houthi takeover in Yemen">Houthis took over the Yemeni government</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sana%27a" title="Sana'a">Sana'a</a>, which led to the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Crisis_(2011%E2%80%93present)" title="Yemeni Crisis (2011âpresent)">Saudi Arabian-backed government</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd_Rabbuh_Mansur_Hadi" title="Abd Rabbuh Mansur Hadi">Abd Rabbuh Mansur Hadi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nevola-Shiban_2020_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nevola-Shiban_2020-139">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glenn_2015_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn_2015-140">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-government_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-government-141">[140]</a></sup> Houthis and their allies gained control of a significant part of Yemen's territory, and resisted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabian-led_intervention_in_Yemen" title="Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen">Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen</a> seeking to restore Hadi in power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nevola-Shiban_2020_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nevola-Shiban_2020-139">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glenn_2015_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn_2015-140">[139]</a></sup> (<i>See</i>: <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Saudi_Arabia_proxy_conflict" title="IranâSaudi Arabia proxy conflict">IranâSaudi Arabia proxy conflict</a>). Both the Houthis and the Saudi Arabian-led coalition were being attacked by the SunnÄ« <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> <a href="/wiki/Militant" title="Militant">militant</a> group and <a href="/wiki/Salafi_jihadism" title="Salafi jihadism">Salafi-jihadist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrorist_organization" title="Terrorist organization">terrorist organization</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIL/ISIS/IS/Daesh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljarevic_2021_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poljarevic_2021-144">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rickenbacher_2019_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rickenbacher_2019-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Badara_2017_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Badara_2017-146">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bunzel_2015_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bunzel_2015-147">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Shia_Islam" title="History of Shia Islam">History of Shia Islam</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_Shia_Islam" title="Origin of Shia Islam">Origin of Shia Islam</a></div>
<h3><span id="Succession_of_.CA.BFAl.C4.AB"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_of_Ê¿AlÄ«">Succession of Ê¿AlÄ«</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Shia_view_of_Ali" title="Shia view of Ali">Shia view of Ali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">Succession to Muhammad</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ali_in_the_Quran" title="Ali in the Quran">Ali in the Quran</a></div>
<p>ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims believe that just as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">prophet</a> is appointed by <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> alone, only God has the prerogative to appoint the successor to his prophet. They believe God chose <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« á¹¬Älib</a> to be Muhammad's successor, infallible, the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a> (<i>khalÄ«fa</i>, <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a>) of Islam. ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims believe that Muhammad designated Ali as his successor by God's command (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_event_of_Ghadir_Khumm" title="The event of Ghadir Khumm">Eid Al Ghadir</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> Ê¿AlÄ« was Muhammad's first-cousin and closest living male relative as well as his son-in-law, having married Muhammad's daughter, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimah" title="Fatimah">FÄá¹­imah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525-150">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito,_John_2002._p._46_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito,_John_2002._p._46-151">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="The_Party_of_.CA.BFAl.C4.AB"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Party_of_Ê¿AlÄ«">The Party of Ê¿AlÄ«</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/The_Four_Companions" title="The Four Companions">The Four Companions</a></div>
<p>Even during the time of Muhammad, there were signs of split among the companions with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salman_al-Farsi" title="Salman al-Farsi">Salman al-Farsi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dharr_al-Ghifari" title="Abu Dharr al-Ghifari">Abu Dharr al-Ghifari</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miqdad" title="Miqdad">Miqdad</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ammar_ibn_Yasir" title="Ammar ibn Yasir">Ammar ibn Yasir</a> amongst the most vehement and loyal supporters of Ê¿AlÄ«.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enc2_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-enc2-152">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rasul_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-rasul-153">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_event_of_Dhul_Asheera">The event of Dhul Asheera</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadith_of_Warning" title="Hadith of Warning">Hadith of Warning</a></div>
<p>During the revelation of <a href="/wiki/Ash-Shu%27ara" title="Ash-Shu'ara">Ash-Shu'ara</a>, the twenty-sixth <a href="/wiki/Surah" title="Surah">Surah</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 617 CE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup> Muhammad is said to have received instructions to warn his family members against adhering to their <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Religion in pre-Islamic Arabia">pre-Islamic religious practices</a>. There are differing accounts of Muhammad's attempt to do this, with one version stating that he had invited his relatives to a meal (later termed the Feast of Dhul Asheera), during which he gave the pronouncement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Ishaq" title="Ibn Ishaq">Ibn Ishaq</a>, it consisted of the following speech:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a> has commanded me to invite you to His religion by saying: And warn thy nearest kinsfolk. I, therefore, warn you, and call upon you to testify that there is no god but Allah, and that I am His messenger. O ye sons of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdul_Muttalib" title="Abdul Muttalib">Abdul Muttalib</a>, no one ever came to you before with anything better than what I have brought to you. By accepting it, your welfare will be assured in this world and in the <a href="/wiki/Jannah" title="Jannah">Hereafter</a>. Who among you will support me in carrying out this momentous duty? Who will share the burden of this work with me? Who will respond to my call? Who will become my vicegerent, my deputy and my <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">wazir</a>?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Among those gathered, only Ê¿AlÄ« offered his consent. Some sources, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Musnad_Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Musnad Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Musnad Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a></i>, do not record Muhammad's reaction to this, though Ibn Ishaq continues that he then declared Ê¿AlÄ« to be his brother, heir and successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> In another narration, when Muhammad accepted Ê¿AlÄ«'s offer, he "threw up his arms around the generous youth, and pressed him to his bosom" and said, "Behold my brother, my vizir, my vicegerentÂ ... let all listen to his words, and obey him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> The direct appointment of Ê¿AlÄ« as heir in this version is notable in that it alleges that his right to succession was established at the very beginning of Muhammad's prophetic activity. The association with the revelation of a Quranic verse also serves the purpose of providing the nomination with authenticity as well as a divine authorization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Event_of_Ghadir_Khumm">Event of Ghadir Khumm</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Event_of_Ghadir_Khumm" title="Event of Ghadir Khumm">Event of Ghadir Khumm</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith"><i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> report</a> of Ghadir Khumm has many different variations and is transmitted by both SunnÄ« and ShÄ«Ê¿a sources. The narrations generally state that in March 632, Muhammad, while returning from his <a href="/wiki/Farewell_Pilgrimage" title="Farewell Pilgrimage">Farewell Pilgrimage</a> alongside a large number of followers and companions, stopped at the oasis of Ghadir Khumm. There, he took Ê¿AlÄ«'s hand and addressed the gathering. The point of contention between different sects arises when Muhammad, whilst giving his speech, gave the proclamation "Anyone who has me as his <i><a href="/wiki/Mawla" title="Mawla">mawla</a></i>, has Ê¿AlÄ« as his <i>mawla.</i>" Some versions add the additional sentence "O God, befriend the friend of Ê¿AlÄ« and be the enemy of his enemy."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amir-Moezzi_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amir-Moezzi-160">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Mawla</i> has a number of meanings in <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, with interpretations of Muhammad's use here being split along sectarian lines between the SunnÄ« and ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims. Among the former group, the word is translated as "friend" or "one who is loyal/close" and that Muhammad was advocating that Ê¿AlÄ« was deserving of friendship and respect. Conversely, ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims tend to view the meaning as being "master" or "ruler",<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and that the statement was a clear designation of Ê¿AlÄ« being Muhammad's appointed successor. ShÄ«Ê¿a sources also record further details of the event, such as stating that those present congratulated Ê¿AlÄ« and acclaimed him as <i><a href="/wiki/Amir_al-Mu%27minin" title="Amir al-Mu'minin">Amir al-Mu'minin</a></i> ("commander of the believers").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amir-Moezzi_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Amir-Moezzi-160">[159]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Caliphate_of_.CA.BFAl.C4.AB"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Caliphate_of_Ê¿AlÄ«">Caliphate of Ê¿AlÄ«</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Investiture_of_Ali_Edinburgh_codex.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="796" data-file-width="624" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Investiture_of_Ali_Edinburgh_codex.jpg/220px-Investiture_of_Ali_Edinburgh_codex.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Investiture_of_Ali_Edinburgh_codex.jpg/330px-Investiture_of_Ali_Edinburgh_codex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Investiture_of_Ali_Edinburgh_codex.jpg/440px-Investiture_of_Ali_Edinburgh_codex.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Investiture_of_Ali_Edinburgh_codex.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The investiture of <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« á¹¬Älib</a> at <a href="/wiki/Rabigh#Ghadir_Al-Khumm" title="Rabigh">Ghadir Khumm</a> (<a href="/wiki/The_Remaining_Signs_of_Past_Centuries" title="The Remaining Signs of Past Centuries">MS Arab 161</a>, fol. 162r, 1308â1309 CE, <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanid</a> manuscript illustration)</div></div></div>
<p>When Muhammad died in 632 CE, <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« á¹¬Älib</a> and Muhammad's closest relatives made the funeral arrangements. While they were preparing his body, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">AbÅ« Bakr</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khattab" title="Umar ibn al-Khattab">Ê¿Umar ibn al-Khaá¹­á¹­Äb</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Ubaidah_ibn_al_Jarrah" title="Abu Ubaidah ibn al Jarrah">Abu Ubaidah ibn al Jarrah</a> met with the leaders of Medina and elected AbÅ« Bakr as caliph. Ê¿AlÄ« did not accept the caliphate of AbÅ« Bakr and refused to pledge allegiance to him. This is indicated in a <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith"><i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> report</a> which both SunnÄ« and ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims regard as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahih" title="Sahih">sahih</a></i> (authentic).
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Qutaybah" title="Ibn Qutaybah">Ibn Qutaybah</a>, a 9th-century SunnÄ« Islamic scholar narrates of Ê¿AlÄ«:</p><blockquote><p>I am the servant of God and the brother of the Messenger of God. I am thus more worthy of this office than you. I shall not give allegiance to you [Abu Bakr &amp; Umar] when it is more proper for you to give bayÊ¼ah to me. You have seized this office from the Ansar using your tribal relationship to the Prophet as an argument against them. Would you then seize this office from us, the ahl al-bayt by force? Did you not claim before the Ansar that you were more worthy than they of the caliphate because Muhammad came from among you (but Muhammad was never from Abu Bakr's family) â and thus they gave you leadership and surrendered command? I now contend against you with the same argumentâ¦It is we who are more worthy of the Messenger of God, living or dead. Give us our due right if you truly have faith in God, or else bear the charge of wilfully doing wrong... Umar, I will not yield to your commands: I shall not pledge loyalty to him.' Ultimately Abu Bakr said, "O 'Ali! If you do not desire to give your bay'ah, I am not going to force you for the same.</p></blockquote>
<p>Ê¿AlÄ«'s wife and daughter of Muhammad, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimah" title="Fatimah">FÄá¹­imah</a>, refused to pledge allegiance to <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">AbÅ« Bakr</a> and remained angry with him until she died due to the issues of <a href="/wiki/Fadak" title="Fadak">Fadak</a>, the inheritance from her father, and the situation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attack_on_Fatimah%27s_house" title="Attack on Fatimah's house">Ê¿Umar at FÄá¹­imah's house</a>; this is stated in various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_hadith_collections" title="List of hadith collections">SunnÄ« <i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> collections</a>, including <i><a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Sahih al-Bukhari</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sahih_Muslim" title="Sahih Muslim">Sahih Muslim</a></i>. FÄá¹­imah never pledged allegiance to AbÅ« Bakr; neither did she acknowledge or accept his claim to the caliphate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup> Almost all members of <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hashim" title="Banu Hashim">Banu Hashim</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quraysh_(tribe)" title="Quraysh (tribe)">Quraysh</a> tribe to which Muhammad belonged, and many of <a href="/wiki/Companions_of_the_Prophet" title="Companions of the Prophet">his closest companions</a> (the <i>á¹£aá¸¥Äba</i>) had supported Ê¿AlÄ«'s cause after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_of_Muhammad" title="Death of Muhammad">death of Muhammad</a>, whilst others supported AbÅ« Bakr.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kufa_Mosque_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Kufa_Mosque_1.jpg/240px-Kufa_Mosque_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Kufa_Mosque_1.jpg/360px-Kufa_Mosque_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Kufa_Mosque_1.jpg/480px-Kufa_Mosque_1.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kufa_Mosque_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kufa" title="Great Mosque of Kufa">Great Mosque of Kufa</a>, site of Ê¿AlÄ«'s assassination (661 CE)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525-150">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito,_John_2002._p._46_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito,_John_2002._p._46-151">[150]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:InsideImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="990" data-file-width="1404" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/InsideImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG/240px-InsideImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/InsideImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG/360px-InsideImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/InsideImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG/480px-InsideImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:InsideImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zarih" title="Zarih">á¸arÄ«áº</a> over Ê¿AlÄ«'s <i>qabr</i> (grave), <a href="/wiki/Imam_Ali_Shrine" title="Imam Ali Shrine">Sanctuary of ImÄm Ê¿AlÄ«</a>, <a href="/wiki/Najaf" title="Najaf">Najaf</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>It was not until the murder of the third <i><a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">rÄshidÅ«n</a></i> caliph, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_ibn_Affan" title="Uthman ibn Affan">Ê¿UthmÄn</a> (657 CE), that the Muslims of <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> in desperation invited Ê¿AlÄ« to become the fourth caliph as the last source,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525-150">[149]</a></sup> and he established his capital in <a href="/wiki/Kufa" title="Kufa">Kufa</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica738_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica738-15">[15]</a></sup> Ê¿AlÄ«'s rule over the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">early Muslim community</a> was often contested, and wars were waged against him. As a result, he had to struggle to maintain his power against the groups who betrayed him after giving allegiance to his succession, or those who wished to take his position. This dispute eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/First_Fitna" title="First Fitna">First Fitna</a>, which was the first major <a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">civil war</a> between Muslims within the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">early Islamic empire</a>. The First Fitna began as a series of revolts fought against Ê¿AlÄ«, caused by the assassination of his political predecessor, Ê¿UthmÄn. While the rebels had previously affirmed the legitimacy of Ê¿AlÄ«'s <i>khilafÄÊ¾</i> (caliphate), they later turned against Ê¿AlÄ« and fought him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525_150-3"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525-150">[149]</a></sup> Ê¿AlÄ« ruled from 656 CE to 661 CE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525_150-4"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-Webster_1999,_p._525-150">[149]</a></sup> when he was assassinated<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Esposito,_John_2002._p._46_151-2"><a href="#cite_note-Esposito,_John_2002._p._46-151">[150]</a></sup> while prostrating in prayer (<i><a href="/wiki/Sujud" title="Sujud">sujud</a></i>). Ê¿AlÄ«'s main rival, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Muawiyah</a>, then claimed the caliphate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>The connection between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley" title="Indus Valley">Indus Valley</a> and ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam was established through the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">early Muslim conquests</a>. According to Derryl N. Maclean, a link between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_invasion_of_India" title="Muslim invasion of India">Sindh region</a> and ShÄ«Ê¿as or proto-ShÄ«Ê¿as can be traced to Hakim ibn Jabalah al-Abdi, a <a href="/wiki/Companions_of_the_Prophet" title="Companions of the Prophet">companion of Muhammad</a>, who traveled across the Sindh to <a href="/wiki/Makran" title="Makran">Makran</a> in the year 649 CE, and presented a report on the area to the caliph. He supported Ê¿AlÄ«, and died in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Camel" title="Battle of the Camel">Battle of the Camel</a> alongside Sindhi <a href="/wiki/Jats" title="Jats">Jats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup> He was also a poet and few couplets of his poem in praise of Ê¿AlÄ« have survived, as reported in Chachnama:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"Oh Ali, owing to your alliance (with the prophet) you are truly of high birth, and your example is great, and you are wise and excellent, and your advent has made your age an age of generosity and kindness and brotherly love".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup> </p></blockquote>
<p>During the caliphate of Ê¿AlÄ«, many Jats came under the influence of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup> Harith ibn Murrah Al-abdi and Sayfi ibn Fil' al-Shaybani, both officers of Ê¿AlÄ«'s army, attacked Sindhi bandits and chased them to Al-Qiqan (present-day <a href="/wiki/Quetta" title="Quetta">Quetta</a>) in the year 658 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup> Sayfi was one of the seven ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims who were beheaded alongside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hujr_ibn_%27Adi" title="Hujr ibn 'Adi">Hujr ibn Adi al-Kindi</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> in 660 CE, near <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id=".E1.B8.A4asan_ibn_.CA.BFAl.C4.AB"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="á¸¤asan_ibn_Ê¿AlÄ«">á¸¤asan ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hasan ibn Ali</a></div>
<p>Upon the death of Ê¿AlÄ«, his elder son <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">á¸¤asan</a> became leader of the Muslims of Kufa, and after a series of skirmishes between the Kufa Muslims and the army of Muawiyah, á¸¤asan agreed to cede the caliphate to Muawiyah and maintain peace among Muslims upon certain conditions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>The <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_tradition_of_cursing_Ali" title="Umayyad tradition of cursing Ali">enforced public cursing of Ê¿AlÄ«</a>, e.g. during prayers, should be abandoned</li>
<li>Muawiyah should not use tax money for his own private needs</li>
<li>There should be peace, and followers of á¸¤asan should be given security and their rights</li>
<li>Muawiyah will never adopt the title of <i><a href="/wiki/Amir_al-Mu%27minin" title="Amir al-Mu'minin">Amir al-Mu'minin</a></i> ("commander of the believers")</li>
<li>Muawiyah will not nominate any successor</li></ol>
<p>á¸¤asan then retired to <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, where in 670 CE he was poisoned by his wife Ja'da bint al-Ash'ath ibn Qays, after being secretly contacted by Muawiyah who wished to pass the caliphate to his own son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yazid_ibn_Mu%27awiyah" title="Yazid ibn Mu'awiyah">Yazid</a> and saw á¸¤asan as an obstacle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id=".E1.B8.A4usayn_ibn_.CA.BFAl.C4.AB"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="á¸¤usayn_ibn_Ê¿AlÄ«">á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">Husayn ibn Ali</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Battle_of_Karbala_-_Abbas_Al-Musavi_-_overall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="826" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Battle_of_Karbala_-_Abbas_Al-Musavi_-_overall.jpg/250px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Battle_of_Karbala_-_Abbas_Al-Musavi_-_overall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Battle_of_Karbala_-_Abbas_Al-Musavi_-_overall.jpg/375px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Battle_of_Karbala_-_Abbas_Al-Musavi_-_overall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Battle_of_Karbala_-_Abbas_Al-Musavi_-_overall.jpg/500px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Battle_of_Karbala_-_Abbas_Al-Musavi_-_overall.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Battle_of_Karbala_-_Abbas_Al-Musavi_-_overall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Battle of Karbala</i>, painting by the <a href="/wiki/Isfahan" title="Isfahan">Isfahan</a>-based Persian artist Abbas Al-Mousavi, <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a> (between 1868 and 1933).</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a>, Ê¿AlÄ«'s younger son and brother to á¸¤asan, initially resisted calls to lead the Muslims against Muawiyah and reclaim the caliphate. In 680 CE, Muawiyah died and passed the caliphate to his son <a href="/wiki/Yazid_I" title="Yazid I">Yazid</a>, and breaking the treaty with á¸¤asan ibn Ê¿AlÄ«. Yazid asked Husayn to swear allegiance (<i><a href="/wiki/Bay%27ah" title="Bay'ah">bay'ah</a></i>) to him. Ê¿AlÄ«'s faction, having expected the caliphate to return to Ê¿AlÄ«'s line upon Muawiyah's death, saw this as a betrayal of the peace treaty and so á¸¤usayn rejected this request for allegiance. There was a groundswell of support in Kufa for á¸¤usayn to return there and take his position as caliph and Imam, so á¸¤usayn collected his family and followers in Medina and set off for Kufa. En route to Kufa, he was blocked by an army of Yazid's men, which included people from Kufa, near Karbala (modern Iraq); á¸¤usayn and approximately 72 of his family members and followers were killed in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Karbala" title="Battle of Karbala">Battle of Karbala</a>.
</p>
