Husayn Mosque">Mosque of Imam á¸¤usayn</a> premises during <a href="/wiki/Arba%27een" title="Arba'een">Arba'een</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<p>ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims regard á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ« as a martyr (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shahid" title="Shahid">shahid</a></i>), and count him as an Imam from the <i>Ahl al-Bayt</i>. They view á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ« as the defender of Islam from annihilation at the hands of <a href="/wiki/Yazid_I" title="Yazid I">Yazid I</a>. á¸¤usayn is the last Imam following Ê¿AlÄ« mutually recognized by all branches of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> The Battle of Karbala and martyrdom of á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ« is often cited as the <a href="/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations" title="ShiaâSunni relations">definitive separation between the ShÄ«Ê¿a and SunnÄ« sects of Islam</a>, and is commemorated each year by ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_of_Ashura" title="Day of Ashura">Day of Ashura</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imamate_of_the_Ahl_al-Bayt">Imamate of the <i>Ahl al-Bayt</i></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Shia_doctrine" title="Imamate in Shia doctrine">Imamate in Shia doctrine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sword_and_shield_reproduction_from_Bab_al_Nasr_gate_Cairo_Egypt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="582" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Sword_and_shield_reproduction_from_Bab_al_Nasr_gate_Cairo_Egypt.jpg/220px-Sword_and_shield_reproduction_from_Bab_al_Nasr_gate_Cairo_Egypt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Sword_and_shield_reproduction_from_Bab_al_Nasr_gate_Cairo_Egypt.jpg/330px-Sword_and_shield_reproduction_from_Bab_al_Nasr_gate_Cairo_Egypt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Sword_and_shield_reproduction_from_Bab_al_Nasr_gate_Cairo_Egypt.jpg/440px-Sword_and_shield_reproduction_from_Bab_al_Nasr_gate_Cairo_Egypt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sword_and_shield_reproduction_from_Bab_al_Nasr_gate_Cairo_Egypt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Zulfiqar" title="Zulfiqar">Zulfiqar</a></i> with and without the shield. The <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_art" title="Fatimid art">Fatimid depiction</a> of Ê¿AlÄ«'s sword is carved on the gates of <a href="/wiki/Old_Cairo" title="Old Cairo">Old Cairo</a>, namely <i>Bab al-Nasr</i> (shown below). Two swords were captured from the temple of the <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Religion in pre-Islamic Arabia">pre-Islamic Arabian deity</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man%C4%81t" title="ManÄt">ManÄt</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Raid_of_Sa%27d_ibn_Zaid_al-Ashhali" title="Raid of Sa'd ibn Zaid al-Ashhali">Raid of Sa'd ibn Zaid al-Ashhali</a>. Muhammad gave them to Ê¿AlÄ«, saying that one of them was "Zulfiqar", which became famously known as the sword of Ê¿AlÄ« and a later symbol of ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sword_Zufiqar_on_old_Cairo_wall.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sword_Zufiqar_on_old_Cairo_wall.jpeg/220px-Sword_Zufiqar_on_old_Cairo_wall.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sword_Zufiqar_on_old_Cairo_wall.jpeg/330px-Sword_Zufiqar_on_old_Cairo_wall.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Sword_Zufiqar_on_old_Cairo_wall.jpeg/440px-Sword_Zufiqar_on_old_Cairo_wall.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sword_Zufiqar_on_old_Cairo_wall.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of Ê¿AlÄ«'s sword and shield carved on the <i>Bab al-Nasr</i> gate wall in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a></div></div></div>
<p>Later, most denominations of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelvers" title="Twelvers">Twelvers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ«s</a>, became <a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Shia_doctrine" title="Imamate in Shia doctrine">Imamis</a>. Imami ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«tes believe that Imams are the spiritual and political <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">successors to Muhammad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-183">[182]</a></sup> Imams are human individuals who not only rule over the Muslim community with justice, but also are able to keep and interpret the divine law and its <a href="/wiki/Esoteric_interpretation_of_the_Quran" title="Esoteric interpretation of the Quran">esoteric meaning</a>. The words and deeds of Muhammad and the Imams are a guide and model for the community to follow; as a result, they must be free from error and sin, and must be chosen by <a href="/wiki/Nass_(Islam)" title="Nass (Islam)">divine decree</a> (<i>nass</i>) through Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nasr_a_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nasr_a-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Momen_1985,_p._174_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Momen_1985,_p._174-102">[101]</a></sup> According to this view peculiar to ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam, there is always an Imam of the Age, who is the divinely appointed authority on all matters of faith and law in the Muslim community. Ê¿AlÄ« was the first Imam of this line, the rightful successor to Muhammad, followed by male descendants of Muhammad through his daughter Fatimah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-183">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>This difference between following either the <i><a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">Ahl al-Bayt</a></i> (Muhammad's family and descendants) or pledging allegiance to AbÅ« Bakr has shaped the <a href="/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations" title="ShiaâSunni relations">ShÄ«Ê¿aâSunnÄ« divide</a> on the interpretation of some Quranic verses, <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith"><i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> literature</a> (accounts of the sayings and living habits attributed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_prophet" title="Islamic prophet">Islamic prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> during his lifetime), and other areas of Islamic belief throughout the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">history of Islam</a>. For instance, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_hadith_collections" title="List of hadith collections"><i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> collections</a> venerated by ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims are centered on narrations by members of the <i>Ahl al-Bayt</i> and their supporters, while some <i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> transmitted by narrators not belonging to or supporting the <i>Ahl al-Bayt</i> are not included. Those of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hurairah" title="Abu Hurairah">Abu Hurairah</a>, for example, Ibn Asakir in his <i>TaÊ¿rikh Kabir</i>, and Muttaqi in his <i>KanzuÊ¿l-Umma</i> report that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_al-Khattab" title="Umar ibn al-Khattab">Ê¿Umar ibn al-Khaá¹­á¹­Äb</a> lashed him, rebuked him, and forbade him to narrate <i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> from Muhammad. Ê¿Umar is reported to have said: "Because you narrate hadith in large numbers from the Holy Prophet, you are fit only for attributing lies to him. (That is, one expects a wicked man like you to utter only lies about the Holy Prophet.) So you must stop narrating hadith from the Prophet; otherwise, I will send you to the land of Dus." (An <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Arabia" title="Tribes of Arabia">Arab clan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, to which Abu Hurairah belonged). According to SunnÄ« Muslims, Ê¿AlÄ« was the fourth successor to AbÅ« Bakr, while ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims maintain that Ê¿AlÄ« was the first divinely sanctioned "Imam", or successor of Muhammad. The seminal event in ShÄ«Ê¿a history is the martyrdom at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Karbala" title="Battle of Karbala">Battle of Karbala</a> of Ê¿AlÄ«'s son, <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a>, and 71 of his followers in 680 CE, who led a non-allegiance movement against the defiant caliph.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Imam_reza_shrine_in_Mashhad_(Longitudinal_Cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1110" data-file-width="871" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Imam_reza_shrine_in_Mashhad_%28Longitudinal_Cropped%29.jpg/230px-Imam_reza_shrine_in_Mashhad_%28Longitudinal_Cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Imam_reza_shrine_in_Mashhad_%28Longitudinal_Cropped%29.jpg/345px-Imam_reza_shrine_in_Mashhad_%28Longitudinal_Cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Imam_reza_shrine_in_Mashhad_%28Longitudinal_Cropped%29.jpg/460px-Imam_reza_shrine_in_Mashhad_%28Longitudinal_Cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Imam_reza_shrine_in_Mashhad_(Longitudinal_Cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imam_Reza_Shrine" title="Imam Reza Shrine">Sanctuary of Imam Reza</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mashhad" title="Mashhad">Mashhad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, is a complex which contains the mausoleum of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imam_Reza" title="Imam Reza">Imam Reza</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Imams" title="Twelve Imams">8th Imam</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿as</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>It is believed in Twelver and IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« branches of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam that <a href="/wiki/%27Aql" title="'Aql">divine wisdom</a> (<i>Ê¿aql</i>) was the source of the souls of the prophets and Imams, which bestowed upon them <a href="/wiki/Hikmah" title="Hikmah">esoteric knowledge</a> (<i>á¸¥ikmah</i>), and that their sufferings were a means of divine grace to their devotees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[185]</a></sup> Although the Imam was not the recipient of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahy" title="Wahy">divine revelation</a> (<i>waá¸¥y</i>), he had a close relationship with <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>, through which God guides him, and the Imam, in turn, guides the people. <a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Shia_doctrine" title="Imamate in Shia doctrine">Imamate</a>, or belief in the divine guide, is a fundamental belief in the Twelver and IsmÄÊ¿Ä«lÄ« branches of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam, and is based on the concept that God would not leave humanity without access to divine guidance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Imamat_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Imamat-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Imam_Mahdi.2C_last_Imam_of_the_Sh.C4.AB.CA.BFa"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Imam_Mahdi,_last_Imam_of_the_ShÄ«Ê¿a">Imam Mahdi, last Imam of the ShÄ«Ê¿a</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad al-Mahdi">Muhammad al-Mahdi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Occultation_(Islam)" title="Occultation (Islam)">Occultation (Islam)</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reappearance_of_Hujjat_Allah_al-Mahdi" title="Reappearance of Hujjat Allah al-Mahdi">Reappearance of Hujjat Allah al-Mahdi</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Shia_Islam" title="History of Shia Islam">History of Shia Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imamate_in_Shia_doctrine" title="Imamate in Shia doctrine">Imamate in Shia doctrine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ghazan_et_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="461" data-file-width="418" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Ghazan_et_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpg/250px-Ghazan_et_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Ghazan_et_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpg/375px-Ghazan_et_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Ghazan_et_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ghazan_et_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ghazan" title="Ghazan">Ghazan</a> and his brother <a href="/wiki/%C3%96ljait%C3%BC" title="ÃljaitÃ¼">ÃljaitÃ¼</a> both were tolerant of <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">sectarian differences within the boundaries of Islam</a>, in contrast to the traditions of <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam, Imam <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a> is regarded as the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">prophesied eschatological redeemer of Islam</a> who will rule for seven, nine, or nineteen years (according to differing interpretations) before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_of_Judgment" title="Day of Judgment">Day of Judgment</a> and will rid the world of evil. According to Islamic tradition, the Mahdi's tenure will coincide with the <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Jesus</a> (Ê¿ÄªsÄ), who is to assist the Mahdi against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masih_ad-Dajjal" title="Masih ad-Dajjal">Masih ad-Dajjal</a> (literally, the "false Messiah" or Antichrist). Jesus, who is considered the <i>Masih</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>") in Islam, will descend at the point of a white arcade east of <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, dressed in yellow robes with his head anointed. He will then join the Mahdi in his war against the Dajjal, where it is believed the Mahdi will slay the Dajjal and unite humankind.
</p><p>Historians dispute over the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Shia_Islam" title="History of Shia Islam">origins of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam</a>, with many Western scholars positing that ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm began as a political faction rather than a truly religious movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-franc23_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-franc23-188">[187]</a></sup> Other scholars disagree, considering this concept of religious-political separation to be an anachronistic application of a Western concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dynasties">Dynasties</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Shia_dynasties" title="List of Shia dynasties">List of Shia dynasties</a></div>
<p>In the century following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Karbala" title="Battle of Karbala">Battle of Karbala</a> (680 CE), as various Shia-affiliated groups diffused in the emerging Islamic world, several nations arose based on a Shia leadership or population.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Idrisid_dynasty" title="Idrisid dynasty">Idrisids</a> (788â985 CE): a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zaydi" title="Zaydi">Zaydi</a> dynasty in what is now Morocco</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Qarmatians" title="Qarmatians">Qarmatians</a> (899â1077 CE): an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ismaili" title="Ismaili">Ismaili</a> <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> dynasty. Their headquarters were in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Arabia" title="East Arabia">East Arabia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>. It was founded by <a href="/wiki/Abu_Sa%27id_al-Jannabi" title="Abu Sa'id al-Jannabi">Abu Sa'id al-Jannabi</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Buyid_dynasty" title="Buyid dynasty">Buyids</a> (934â1055 CE): a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver</a> <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> dynasty. at its peak consisted of large portions of modern Iraq and Iran.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uqaylid_Dynasty" title="Uqaylid Dynasty">Uqaylids</a> (990â1096 CE): a Shia <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> dynasty with several lines that ruled in various parts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Jazira,_Mesopotamia" title="Al-Jazira, Mesopotamia">Al-Jazira</a>, northern Syria and Iraq.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> (1256â1335): a <a href="/wiki/Persianate_society" title="Persianate society">Persianate</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol" title="Mongol">Mongol</a> <a href="/wiki/Khanate" title="Khanate">khanate</a> established in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> in the 13th century, considered a part of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>. The Ilkhanate was based, originally, on <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>'s campaigns in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwarezmid_Empire" title="Khwarezmid Empire">Khwarezmid Empire</a> in 1219â1224, and founded by Genghis's grandson, <a href="/wiki/Hulagu_Khan" title="Hulagu Khan">Hulagu</a>, in territories which today comprise most of Iran, Iraq, Afghanistan, Turkmenistan, Armenia, Azerbaijan, Georgia, Turkey, and Pakistan. The Ilkhanate initially embraced many religions, but was particularly sympathetic to Buddhism and Christianity. Later Ilkhanate rulers, beginning with <a href="/wiki/Ghazan" title="Ghazan">Ghazan</a> in 1295, embraced Islam his brother <a href="/wiki/%C3%96ljait%C3%BC" title="ÃljaitÃ¼">ÃljaitÃ¼</a> promoted Shia Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[189]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bahmani_Sultanate" title="Bahmani Sultanate">Bahmanids</a> (1347â1527): a Shia Muslim state of the <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Deccan</a> in southern India and one of the great medieval Indian kingdoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup> Bahmanid Sultanate was the first independent Islamic Kingdom in South India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ansari_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ansari-192">[191]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="983" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/220px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/330px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/440px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a> at its peak</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fatimid_Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:El_Hakim_Mosque.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3454" data-file-width="5183" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/El_Hakim_Mosque.jpg/260px-El_Hakim_Mosque.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/El_Hakim_Mosque.jpg/390px-El_Hakim_Mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/El_Hakim_Mosque.jpg/520px-El_Hakim_Mosque.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:El_Hakim_Mosque.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Al Hakim Mosque, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Cairo" title="Islamic Cairo">Islamic Cairo</a>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimids</a> (909â1171 CE): Controlled much of North Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>. The group takes its name from Fatima, Muhammad's daughter, from whom they claim descent.</li>
<li>In 909 CE the Shi'i military leader <a href="/wiki/Abu_Abdallah_al-Shi%27i" title="Abu Abdallah al-Shi'i">Abu Abdallah al-ShiÊ»i</a>, overthrew the Sunni ruler in Northern Africa; which began the Fatimid regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-193">[192]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jawhar_(general)" title="Jawhar (general)">Jawhar (general)</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">Ø¬ÙÙØ±</span>; <abbr title="floruit ('flourished'Â â known to have been active at a particular time or during a particular period)">fl.</abbr> 966âd. 992) was a <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid</a> general. Under the command of Caliph <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27izz_li-Din_Allah" title="Al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah">Al-Mu'izz</a>, he led the conquest of <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and then of Egypt,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup> founded the city of <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup> and the great <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_Mosque" title="Al-Azhar Mosque">al-Azhar Mosque</a>. A Greek slave by origin, he was freed by Al-Mu'izz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Safavid_Empire">Safavid Empire</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_to_Shia_Islam" title="Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam">Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Ismail_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="993" data-file-width="776" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Shah_Ismail_I.jpg/220px-Shah_Ismail_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Shah_Ismail_I.jpg/330px-Shah_Ismail_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Shah_Ismail_I.jpg/440px-Shah_Ismail_I.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shah_Ismail_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the first actions performed by <a href="/wiki/List_of_monarchs_of_Persia" title="List of monarchs of Persia">ShÄh</a> <a href="/wiki/Ismail_I" title="Ismail I">IsmÄ'Ä«l I</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> was the proclamation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelver</a> denomination of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam as the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_conversion_of_Iran_to_Shia_Islam" title="Safavid conversion of Iran to Shia Islam">official religion</a> of his newly-founded <a href="/wiki/Safavid_Iran" title="Safavid Iran">Persian Empire</a>, causing sectarian tensions in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> when he destroyed the tombs of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Abbasid_caliphs" title="List of Abbasid caliphs">Abbasid caliphs</a>, the SunnÄ« Imam <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Hanifa_an-Nu%27man" title="Abu Hanifa an-Nu'man">AbÅ« á¸¤anÄ«fa al-NuÊ¿mÄn</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">á¹¢Å«fÄ« Muslim</a> ascetic <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Qadir_Gilani" title="Abdul Qadir Gilani">Ê¿Abdul QÄdir GÄ«lÄnÄ«</a> in 1508.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masters_2009_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Masters_2009-197">[196]</a></sup> In 1533 the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottoman Turks</a>, upon their conquest of <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, rebuilt various important SunnÄ« shrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Masters_2009_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-Masters_2009-197">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A major turning point in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Shia_Islam" title="History of Shia Islam">history of ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam</a> was the dominion of the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Safavid dynasty</a> (1501â1736) in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Iran" title="History of Iran">Persia</a>. This caused a number of changes in the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>The ending of the relative mutual tolerance between SunnÄ«s and ShÄ«Ê¿as that existed from the time of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_conquests" title="Mongol conquests">Mongol conquests</a> onwards and the resurgence of antagonism between the two groups.</li>
<li>Initial dependence of <a href="/wiki/Shia_clergy" title="Shia clergy">ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«te clerics</a> on the state followed by the emergence of an independent body of <i><a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">ulama</a></i> capable of taking a political stand different from official policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup></li>
<li>The growth in importance of <a href="/wiki/Safavid_Iran" title="Safavid Iran">Persian</a> centers of Islamic education and religious learning, which resulted in the change of Twelver ShÄ«Ê¿Ä«sm from being a predominantly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_people" title="Arab people">Arab</a> phenomenon to become predominantly Persian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup></li>
<li>The growth of the <a href="/wiki/Akhbari" title="Akhbari">Akhbari</a> school of thought, which taught that only the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith"><i>á¸¥adÄ«th</i> literature</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i> (accounts of the sayings and living habits attributed to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_prophet" title="Islamic prophet">Islamic prophet</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> during his lifetime) are to be bases for verdicts, rejecting the use of reasoning.</li></ul>
<p>With the fall of the Safavids, the state in Persiaâincluding the state system of courts with government-appointed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judge_(Islamic_law)" title="Judge (Islamic law)">judges</a> (<i>qÄá¸Ä«</i>)âbecame much weaker. This gave the <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia"><i>sharÄ«Ê¿a</i> courts</a> of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mujtahid" title="Mujtahid">mujtahid</a></i> an opportunity to fill the legal vacuum and enabled the <i>ulama</i> to assert their judicial authority. The <a href="/wiki/Usuli" title="Usuli">Usuli</a> school of thought also increased in strength at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span id="Persecution_of_Sh.C4.AB.CA.BFa_Muslims"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Persecution_of_ShÄ«Ê¿a_Muslims">Persecution of ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Shiism" title="Anti-Shiism">Anti-Shiism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations" title="ShiaâSunni relations">ShiaâSunni relations</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sectarian_violence_among_Muslims" title="Sectarian violence among Muslims">Sectarian violence among Muslims</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia_genocide" title="Shia genocide">Shia genocide</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="987" data-file-width="1389" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/ImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG/250px-ImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/ImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG/375px-ImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/ImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG/500px-ImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ImamAliMosqueNajafIraq.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Imam_Ali_Shrine" title="Imam Ali Shrine">Shrine of Imam Ê¿AlÄ«</a> in <a href="/wiki/Najaf" title="Najaf">Najaf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a></div></div></div>
<p>The history of <a href="/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations" title="ShiaâSunni relations">ShÄ«Ê¿aâSunnÄ« relations</a> has often involved <a href="/wiki/Religious_discrimination" title="Religious discrimination">religious discrimination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_persecution" title="Religious persecution">persecution</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religious_violence#Islam" title="Religious violence">violence</a>, dating back to the earliest development of the two competing sects.
At various times throughout the history of Islam, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Shiism" title="Anti-Shiism">ShÄ«Ê¿a groups and minorities have faced persecution perpetrated by SunnÄ« Muslims</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p>Militarily established and holding control over the Umayyad government, many SunnÄ« rulers perceived the ShÄ«Ê¿as as a threatâboth to their political and religious authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[205]</a></sup> The SunnÄ« rulers under the Umayyad dynasty sought to marginalize the ShÄ«Ê¿a minority, and later the Abbasids turned on their ShÄ«Ê¿a allies and imprisoned, persecuted, and killed them. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Shiism" title="Anti-Shiism">persecution of ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims throughout history by their SunnÄ« co-religionists</a> has often been characterized by <a href="/wiki/Religious_violence#Islam" title="Religious violence">brutal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocidal</a> acts. Comprising only about 10â15% of the <a href="/wiki/Islam_by_country" title="Islam by country">global Muslim population</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PEW2009_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-PEW2009-12">[12]</a></sup> ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims remain a marginalized community to this day in many SunnÄ«-dominant <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab countries</a>, without the rights to practice their religion and freely organize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1514, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty" title="Ottoman dynasty">sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim I</a> (1512â1520) ordered the massacre of 40,000 <a href="/wiki/Alevism" title="Alevism">Alevis</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bektashi" title="Bektashi">Bektashi</a> (Anatolian ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Jalal_Al-e-Ahmad" title="Jalal Al-e-Ahmad">Jalal Al-e-Ahmad</a>, "Sultan Selim I carried things so far that he announced that the killing of one ShÄ«Ê¿a had as much <a href="/wiki/Afterlife#Islam" title="Afterlife">otherworldly reward</a> as killing 70 <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians" title="Persecution of Christians">Christians</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup> In 1802, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saud" title="House of Saud">Al Saud</a>-<a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabi</a> armies of the <a href="/wiki/Ikhwan" title="Ikhwan">Ikhwan</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Diriyah" title="Emirate of Diriyah">First Saudi State</a> (1727â1818) <a href="/wiki/Wahhabi_sack_of_Karbala" title="Wahhabi sack of Karbala">attacked and sacked the city of Karbala</a>, the ShÄ«Ê¿a shrine in <a href="/wiki/Najaf" title="Najaf">Najaf</a> (eastern region of Iraq) that commemorates the martyrdom and death of <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">á¸¤usayn ibn Ê¿AlÄ«</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athist_Iraq" title="Ba'athist Iraq">Ba'athist regime in Iraq</a> (1968â2003), ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims were heavily persecuted, arrested, tortured, and killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup> In March 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Malaysia" title="Government of Malaysia">Malaysian government</a> declared ShÄ«Ê¿a Islam a "deviant" sect and banned ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims from promoting their faith to other Muslims, but left them free to practice it themselves privately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most recent and grave attempt by SunnÄ« Muslims to entirely eradicate the ShÄ«Ê¿a community through violent means was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia_genocide" title="Shia genocide">large-scale genocide of ShÄ«Ê¿a Muslims organized and perpetrated by ISIL/ISIS/IS/Daesh</a> in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and Iraq between 2014 and 2018,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljarevic_2021_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Poljarevic_2021-144">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baele_2019_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baele_2019-214">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rickenbacher_2019_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rickenbacher_2019-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup> which occurred alongside the genocides of many other religious minorities in the same region of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> perpetrated by the aforementioned SunnÄ« <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> <a href="/wiki/Militant" title="Militant">militant</a> group and <a href="/wiki/Salafi_jihadism" title="Salafi jihadism">Salafi-jihadist</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrorist_organization" title="Terrorist organization">terrorist organization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poljarevic_2021_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-Poljarevic_2021-144">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rickenbacher_2019_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-Rickenbacher_2019-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Badara_2017_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Badara_2017-146">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bunzel_2015_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bunzel_2015-147">[146]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A 2019 <a href="/wiki/Council_on_Foreign_Relations" title="Council on Foreign Relations">Council on Foreign Relations</a> article states: "Nobody really knows the size of the Shia population in Nigeria. Credible estimates that its numbers range between 2 and 3 percent of Nigeria's population, which would amount to roughly four million."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-85">[85]</a></sup> A 2019 BBC News article said that "Estimates of [Nigerian Shia] numbers vary wildly, ranging from less than 5% to 17% of Nigeria's Muslim population of about 100 million."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></span>
</li>
